SE  House & Home/Style Desk; Section F
HD  A Catalog Entrepreneur Takes the New York Test
BY  By CHRISTOPHER HAWTHORNE
WC 1999 words
PD 3 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 1, Column 2
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

SAN FRANCISCO -- THE topic at Rob Forbes's penthouse on Russian Hill here was supposed to be furniture: to be exact, how Mr. Forbes was planning to fill his converted ballroom atop a building from the 1920's. Given Mr. Forbes's credentials -- he is founder of Design Within Reach, the Internet and catalog retailer of modern furniture whose first New York outlet opened on Saturday -- it seemed reasonable to expect that he might turn his 2,200-square-foot apartment, bought at the end of last year, into a high-design laboratory.

But Mr. Forbes, 51, a potter with an M.B.A. from Stanford, explained that he was concentrating on living in the apartment for a while and absorbing its stunning view of skyscrapers, the Golden Gate and Bay Bridges, sailboats and the distant green hills of Berkeley and Oakland.

TD 

Six weeks after he had moved in, the penthouse was still a little bare, offering a mixture of ad hoc chic and bachelor-pad minimalism. Mr. Forbes had installed some of his own vintage pieces (a small table and chairs by Alvar Aalto, a bench by Hugh Acton and a pair of Pelican chairs by the Danish designer Finn Juhl) and reproductions, including a Noguchi sofa and a red table by Piero Lissoni.

''You know, I really like this place without anything in it,'' he said. ''The space and the view are luxuries on their own.''

Mr. Forbes and his partners take a decidedly more aggressive approach to running Design Within Reach, which offers midcentury classics and pieces by younger designers and has expanded rapidly since its founding. Indeed, even as Design Within Reach continues its Web site (www.dwr.com[http://www.dwr.com/]) and its catalogs -- sending out more than 500,000 each month -- the company has pursued an ambitious expansion into retail outlets over the last 18 months. The 10th Design Within Reach store is a 3,500-square-foot split-level space on Wooster Street in Manhattan designed by the architect Alberto Rivera, who also oversaw the company's new studio in Beverly Hills. Another New York store, in the meatpacking district, and two locations in Florida are also planned.

The stores represent a shift in strategy for a company founded as a streamlined New Economy business in San Francisco in 1999, in the midst of dot-com mania in the Bay Area. Wayne L. Badovinus, chief executive of Design Within Reach, who met Mr. Forbes while both were working for Williams-Sonoma, the housewares company, in the early 1980's, said they had vowed that ''we weren't going into the bricks and mortar business.''

Mr. Forbes said: ''It turns out that a lot of people just like talking to people and the eye contact instead of filling in forms online. For many people, the activity of shopping is a huge pleasure.''

The new Design Within Reach stores appear at a time Mr. Forbes's own profile is rising. Last year he joined the executive board of the International Design Conference in Aspen and served as a juror for Cooper-Hewitt's national design awards and for I.D. magazine's annual design review; he is an increasingly visible presence on the furniture fair circuit.

The Design Within Reach catalog includes an ever-shifting cast of new discoveries to go with its standbys by Ray and Charles Eames and Philippe Starck, reflecting the fruits of Mr. Forbes's frequent research trips. Jennifer Carpenter of Truck Product Architecture in New York said that even when the company was developing its earliest furniture designs, she hoped for a deal with Design Within Reach, as opposed to bigger retailers. Truck Product signed an exclusive deal with the company this year to carry two versions of its 2-Way table, which features a powder-coated lacquer top and a reversible plywood base.

''My goal, absolutely, was Design Within Reach,'' said Ms. Carpenter. ''It's a well-edited collection, full of things that when I was practicing as an architect, I would have specced for a job.''

What the company buys, she said, is news in the design world. ''I've found that people read the catalog like a magazine,'' she said. ''And every architect I know gets it.''

Design Within Reach, whose original backers include Reed Business Information, the publisher of Interior Design magazine, and Jesse Hansen & Co., a San Francisco equity firm, is privately held. Mr. Badovinus declined to say if the company is profitable but confirmed that sales have increased by at least 50 percent in each of the last three years, to about $60 million in 2002.

Despite that growth, the move to New York is a risky one. Their DWR Studio at 142 Wooster Street, near Prince Street, is already open; a sister store is scheduled to open in May at 408 West 14th Street, near Ninth Avenue and the new Vitra store. But Design Within Reach is entering Manhattan in a sluggish economy and a war in Iraq and in the wake of several recent signs of trouble in the furniture business. Herman Miller Inc., based in Michigan, had a 34 percent drop in sales in its 2002 fiscal year, and shuttered its modestly priced Herman Miller Red line.

Other casualties include the modern-furniture retailer Palazzetti, which declared bankruptcy last month after 22 years in business, and Full Upright Position, an online and catalog retailer that provided early competition for Design Within Reach. Sergio Palazzetti, the founder of the Palazzetti chain, worried about the chances of Design Within Reach succeeding with retail outlets. ''They were successful because they had no stores,'' he said, ''no overhead. There are problems to opening a chain of stores and managing locations from a distance. They're changing their formula.''

There are signs, meanwhile, that the revival of midcentury modernism may be tapering off, replaced by a taste for more daring and eclecticism in interior design.

''It's a really tough time in the industry,'' Mr. Forbes acknowledged. ''But we think there's a healthy discipline in operating in an economy where things don't come easily.'' He also suggested that a retail niche big enough to drive an Airstream trailer through remains between the mass market offerings of Ikea and Pottery Barn and high-design boutiques like Moss and outlets of European manufacturers like Kartell and Vitra. Or for that matter like Palazzetti.

Even as it seeks a broader public profile with its retail outlets, Design Within Reach is hoping to solidify its relationship with professionals. This month it is introducing services under the Profile name aimed at professional designers, architects and decorators, a group the company estimates accounts for about half its sales. A new annual catalog, called Profile Workbook, will offer more product details and fewer glamour photographs of the furniture. It will be accompanied by a second DWR Web site (www.dwrprofile.com[http://www.dwrprofile.com/]) and a chatty, tabloid-size magazine, both of which are pitched to the trade. ''We think if we lose that sense of direct connection with professionals, we diminish our place in the market,'' Mr. Badovinus said.

With its $3,795 sofas and $2,300 credenzas, Design Within Reach does not exactly offer rock-bottom prices; indeed, some younger fans of the company in California have nicknamed it DNQWR, for Design Not Quite Within Reach. But the company's combination of competitive pricing and ready-to-ship inventory has helped set it apart.

Keven Wilder, a retail consultant in Chicago, praised the company's strategy. She drew a distinction between the old-school approach to modern furniture, ''where you order it and put a chunk of money down and you wait,'' and a new model, practiced by Design Within Reach, aimed at a younger and less patient consumer. ''These days, especially if people are ordering online, they want it right away,'' Ms. Wilder said.

In its effort to sell classics of midcentury design at relatively low prices, Design Within Reach has navigated some tricky byways of the furniture business. One example is the steel and leather chair created by Ludwig Mies van der Rohe for the German Pavilion at the 1929 International Exhibition in Barcelona.

Knoll, based in Pennsylvania, has been the licensed manufacturer of the chair since 1948, and holds a trademark on the Barcelona name. But the design itself reverted to the public domain more than five decades ago.

Manufacturers argue that unlicensed versions of well-known designs cost them millions in revenue each year. In 1998, Herman Miller sued Palazzetti over a famous lounge chair and ottoman by Charles and Ray Eames. A court ruled that Palazzetti could legally sell the chair, but not under the Eames name. Design Within Reach sells a version of the Mies Barcelona chair that is not manufactured by Knoll -- Mr. Forbes says it is made in an Italian factory -- and sidesteps the trademark issue by calling it the Pavilion chair. The Knoll Barcelona chair can sell for $4,500 and take 12 to 14 weeks to be delivered. Design Within Reach charges $1,995 for its Pavilion chair, and ships it within two to four weeks from its warehouse in Union City, Calif.

''We think people come to Knoll for that chair because we worked with Mies and have been careful to conform to the original specifications,'' said Liz Needle, general manager of Knoll Studio. ''Is it frustrating that people sometimes don't buy Knoll quality but think that's what they're getting? Sure.''

Mr. Forbes defends the craftsmanship of his company's Mies chair. He adds that while he was unable to strike a deal with Knoll to carry its products, he has done so with Herman Miller and other well-known manufacturers.

A weekly e-mail newsletter sent to 170,000 subscribers has helped create Mr. Forbes's persona, which hovers somewhere between fan and tastemaker. The electronic dispatches detail his trips to European furniture fairs and architectural landmarks and his visits with designers around the world. Last month, for example, he wrote about chatting with the German lighting designer Ingo Maurer in an Italian trattoria.

Mr. Badovinus, who maintains a low public profile compared with Mr. Forbes, said that the company's customers had responded warmly to Mr. Forbes and his efforts. ''Sometimes modern design can seem cold or hard-edged,'' he said, ''and Rob helps soften that. In essence what he's saying is, 'I'm a design enthusiast, you're a design enthusiast, and isn't this stuff cool?' ''

That attitude was certainly on view in Mr. Forbes's apartment, where he spent several minutes singing the praises of some of his favorite possessions, the Aalto chair (''I love the way the back connects'') and Acton bench (''See those feet on it, how they look like they're walking?''), before heading into his bedroom.

A patchwork quilt, produced by a group of women in Gee's Bend, Ala., whose work was recently shown at the Whitney Museum, lay across the bed. Almost neon-bright, it offered a jarring contrast to the Oriental carpet on the floor. Mr. Forbes said he bought it from Odd Fellows Antiques, in Maine. ''It's what an Amish quilt would look like if the Amish had more sex,'' he said.

SE  Metropolitan Desk; Section D
HD  Agency Lowers Estimate of Post-9/11 Aid to Small Businesses
BY  By JOSEPH P. FRIED
WC 1234 words
PD 5 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 4, Column 3
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

Prompting the latest dispute over whether government programs are meeting Lower Manhattan's needs since Sept. 11, 2001, New York State's economic development agency has sharply cut its estimate of how much it will spend on a major incentives program for small businesses to stay in the area or move there.

As recently as November, the agency, the Empire State Development Corporation, said the program would probably cost $291 million, but a spokesman said this week that the figure had been ''based on research we did as the program was getting started'' a year ago. Now, he said, the agency believes that the cost will be closer to $155 million, based on how much it has so far committed to pay out: about $50 million to 820 applicants. That appears to meet the needs of eligible applicants, the spokesman, Alex Dudley, said.

TD 

But the cut in the spending projection drew a sharp reaction from critics. They said that $50 million was too small a sum, and that it was small because of overly strict rules that kept the program from providing all the help needed to retain small businesses in Lower Manhattan after the attack destroyed the World Trade Center and left the area economically reeling.

The program, called the Small Firm Attraction and Retention Grant program, began receiving applications in March 2002. The number of small businesses that have applied to it is 1,261, Mr. Dudley said on Tuesday. He said that 238 had been found ineligible and that 203 were awaiting answers.

The program is based in part on the date on which a company signs or renews a lease in Lower Manhattan. The most common reason for the rejections, Mr. Dudley said, is that the businesses fail to meet requirements that their current leases expire no later than Dec. 31, 2004, and that they sign or have signed new or renewal leases after Sept. 11, 2001, and no later than Dec. 31, 2004.

Yet it is those requirements, the critics say, that unreasonably exclude many small businesses in need and that have led the state agency to reduce its projected spending.

It is not the only dispute related to government responsiveness to Lower Manhattan's needs as the area seeks to recover. The Federal Emergency Management Agency, for example, while praised for its prompt payment for the ground zero cleanup, has been criticized for the pace at which it compensated people who suffered economically from 9/11.

The Small Firm Attraction and Retention program is intended to help businesses with up to 200 employees stay in the area below Canal Street or move there.

Mr. Dudley maintained that the program was ''on track.'' His agency says that in the last quarter of 2001 there were about 8,000 Lower Manhattan businesses with up to 200 employees and that an estimated 10 percent renewed their leases annually, roughly approximating the 820 businesses whose applications have been approved since the program began 13 months ago. But Mr. Dudley said he did not have figures on how many of the 820 had moved into Lower Manhattan from elsewhere.

A small-business group, From the Ground Up, which seeks to maximize aid to struggling small companies in the area, says a study it commissioned last year found nearly 13,000 businesses in the area with up to 50 employees each, meaning an even larger number with up to 200.

''I think it's left out a lot of people who are committed to staying downtown,'' said Nancy Ploeger, president of the Manhattan Chamber of Commerce.

Jeannine Chanes, a lawyer for From the Ground Up, said the rules ''do not take into account the economic realities'' of the small-business needs in Lower Manhattan.

Mr. Dudley replied, ''You have to make rules, otherwise you're handing out money with no rhyme or reason.'' He said that the program was succeeding, and that if more than $155 million -- its current allocation -- was needed, his agency would try to get more money from the Lower Manhattan Development Corporation, a subsidiary of Empire State that independently controls $1.3 billion in still uncommitted federal funds for 9/11 aid efforts. ''We have every intention of paying the accepted applicants,'' he said.

The program provides $3,500 or $5,000 a worker to the qualifying business, whose new or renewed leases are required to run at least five years. The amount depends on a business's proximity to the trade center site and where it relocates.

One applicant who will not be paid is Dr. Grace Zaidman, owner of the Broadway Family Dental Center at 287 Broadway. ''I received a letter stating I was rejected because my lease terminates on Aug. 1, 2005,'' after the Dec. 31, 2004, cutoff.

Acceptance of her application, which was based on 11 employees, would have meant close to $40,000 for her over 18 months, assuming the employee number did not change.

Dr. Zaidman said the practice, which has been on the site for 18 years, badly needed the aid. ''Business is still 50 percent off,'' she said, adding that ''481 of our patients died in the World Trade Center.''

Program officials have said a cutoff date was adopted because incentives to keep business in Lower Manhattan or to add to business there were seen as especially important.

Another objection of critics, and even of some otherwise pleased grant recipients, is the rule that in most cases half the total commitment is to be paid when the new or renewal lease takes effect and half 18 months later. The second payment could be larger or smaller, depending on the number of employees at the time. The amount the program has paid out so far is $27 million.

Carlos Gandia, owner of Greenwich Jewelers, which moved to 64 Trinity Place in June 2002, after it could not return to its longtime Greenwich Street location near the trade center site, said the $10,000 that his four-employee shop had already received from the program in June had been helpful.

''But 18 months is a long time,'' he said of the wait for the second installment. ''You can really use it when you first come into a place.''

Mr. Dudley, the spokesman for the Empire State Development Corporation, said: ''We want to encourage not just a short-term commitment. And if businesses grow their employees, they will be eligible for more money in the second payment.''

Richard Cohn, an owner of Southwest New York, a restaurant in the World Financial Center across the street from the trade center site, said he had no quarrel with the program's split payments. The restaurant received about $200,000 as its first installment last summer, based on having about 80 employees.

The program, Mr. Cohn said, shows that ''the government has gone above and beyond in supporting the revitalization of downtown businesses.''

SE  Business/Financial Desk; Section W
HD  Japan Tries A New Tack On Economy: Buying Debt
BY  By KEN BELSON
WC 778 words
PD 9 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 1, Column 6
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

TOKYO, April 8 -- Its arsenal of traditional tools rendered largely ineffective, the Bank of Japan said today that it would consider taking the unconventional step of buying corporate debt to make it easier for small companies to borrow, and in the process battle deflation.

The announcement is the latest in a series of moves by the central bank's new governor, Toshihiko Fukui, responding to pressure from the government to do more to shore up Japan's frail economy. In the month since he took up the bank's top post, Mr. Fukui has demonstrated that he is much more willing than his predecessor, Masaru Hayami, to work with lawmakers and to tinker with the central bank's policies.

TD 

The bank concluded a two-day policy board meeting today, the first of Mr. Fukui's tenure, without taking other steps on monetary policy. The bank already sets short-term interest rates effectively at zero and has been pumping extra liquidity into Japan's money markets in various ways for many months, so there is little left in the standard central-bank playbook for it to try.

Still, the announcement today focused new attention on the plight of small companies in Japan, which have been particularly hard hit by the five-year decline in consumer prices and which tend to be the first customers cut off by banks that are retrenching to clean up their own balance sheets.

Small business is a crucial constituency for the governing Liberal Democratic Party, and lawmakers have often gone to great lengths to help the sector. The government has offered loan guarantees for small companies since 1998. It has also pushed the major commercial banks to step up their small-business lending, with little success.

The central bank said it would consider closing the gap by buying asset-backed securities issued by the banks, including some backed specifically by commercial paper from small companies. Buying these securities relieves the banks of some risk and frees up bank capital that can then be put to use making new loans.

Typically, securitized loans in Japan have been backed by tangible collateral assets like land and buildings. But property values have fallen in Japan for 12 consecutive years, and commercial banks are increasingly reluctant to lend against them.

So the central bank's review will include a type of security common in the United States but not often seen in Japan: one backed by bundles of loans and receivables.

Though the central bank already accepts asset-backed securities as collateral, the ''outright purchase of private debt is an unprecedented measure for a central bank,'' the Bank of Japan said in a statement. The bank's staff is to work out details for the board to discuss at its next meeting in May.

Wary of wrecking its own balance sheet by taking on risky assets, the central bank said that it would consider buying the asset-backed securities only as a temporary measure. Still, it would be a big step away from the conservatism of Mr. Hayami's tenure.

The careful wording of the central bank's announcement is a sign of the political tightrope Mr. Fukui walks. As a longtime central banker, he wants to preserve the bank's independence and maintain continuity with past policies. But he must also face the glum realities of Japan's 12-year economic slide and appease the politicians, including Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi, who named him.

''Today's move is a kind of Band-Aid,'' said Akio Makabe, an economist at the Mizuho Research Institute. ''But Fukui is in a very difficult situation; he has to do something quickly.''

Several economists said that even if the bank decided to go ahead with the proposed debt purchases, that would only patch over a few of the sorest parts of the economy, not solve its broader problems. The most important question now, Mr. Makabe said, is whether the government will respond by announcing new measures to stimulate the economy, or use the bank's move as an excuse to do nothing. Recent history suggests that the latter is more likely.

In fact, leading ruling-party lawmakers are pressing Mr. Koizumi's administration to roll back some of the measures it has already taken to try to bring Japan's financial system up to global standards.

SE  Business/Financial Desk; Section C
HD  Two Start-Ups Aim to Take On Cisco and Juniper
BY  By MATT RICHTEL
WC 383 words
PD 9 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 4, Column 3
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

SAN FRANCISCO, April 8 -- Two Silicon Valley start-ups plan to introduce products on Wednesday that they say will make it less expensive for Internet and telephone companies to route their data traffic.

The products from Procket Networks, based in Milpitas, and Caspian Networks, with headquarters in San Jose, are intended to compete in the router business, a roughly $1.4 billion market dominated by Cisco Systems and Juniper Networks.

TD 

Both Procket Networks and Caspian Networks have piqued the interest of technology industry analysts who say the companies have the potential to take market share from Cisco and Juniper.

''The companies have completely different takes on the market, but they both have the ability to penetrate,'' said Stephen Kamman, a data networking analyst with CIBC World Markets in New York.

He added, ''It'll take 12 to 18 months to know whether they have made any impact.''

Randall Kruep, chief executive of Procket Networks, said the company had been operating in ''stealth mode'' for three and a half years. But he said the company would announce on Wednesday new customers, including NTT/Verio Inc., an Internet service provider.

Mr. Kruep, a former Cisco employee, said Procket Networks had raised $272 million in financing and employs a lot of people from Cisco and Juniper.

He said the company planned to offer routers that are 40 percent to 60 percent less expensive than those offered by the competition.

Caspian Networks said it had raised $262 million in capital and on Wednesday will announce the commercial availability of its new router, the Apeiro. Dallas Kachan, marketing director for Caspian, said the router was created to give service providers greater control over the flow of data. In particular, he said, the routers are designed to guarantee that traffic deemed a high priority will make it through the network first.

Mr. Kamman of CIBC said that the emergence of the two companies might be an indication that telecommunications companies were ready to invest again on their infrastructure, at least in a limited way. That Caspian and Procket have gained early interest suggests the market is not as stagnant as it has been for the last two years, he said.

CLM Patents
SE  Business/Financial Desk; Section C
HD  An inventor offers a salty road map of pitfalls for those looking to bring a product to market.
BY  By Teresa Riordan
WC 870 words
PD 14 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 6, Column 5
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

THREE years ago, Randice-Lisa Altschul was a celebrated entrepreneur and media darling. Her most recent invention -- a disposable cellphone --caught the attention of the BBC, CNBC, ABC, NBC, CBS, Fox, Parade, Glamour, Business Week and The Financial Times of London, all of which interviewed her. She was invited, twice, to lecture at Harvard Business School.

Today, Ms. Altschul's company, Dieceland Technologies, is $2.3 million in the red and it filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection in January. And she is being sued by creditors for control of her patents.

TD 

''Other people might go get drunk and cry in their beer,'' Ms. Altschul said. ''But that's not my style.''

Her style is, rather, to come out swinging. Last month, Ms. Altschul self-published a memoir, ''Financiers, Lawyers, and Other Assorted Snakes: An Entrepreneur's Story,'' which lambastes what she says are the feckless lawyers and fiendish financiers who contributed to her financial demise.

Ms. Altschhul's salty narrative is a road map for inventors looking to bring a product to market. She details what she did right (patented broadly and deeply) and what she did wrong (trusted unscrupulous advisers).

Independent inventors, of course, are notoriously paranoid. But, if Ms. Altschul's account is to be believed, just because you're paranoid does not mean they're not out to get you.

During the initial press frenzy over her invention, she says in her ruminative account, a representative from a large talent agency called, offering to put her in touch with the ''power brokers of power brokers.'' That person turned out to be a financier whom Ms. Altschul calls Dick, who becomes the main villain in her saga. (Under instructions from her lawyers, she used pseudonyms for most of the players.)

In January 2001, she said, ''Dick'' flew in a private plane to meet Ms. Altschul in Texas, where she had flown from her home in Cliffside Park, N.J. While pitching himself to her, she said, ''Dick'' revealed that years earlier he had had a brush with the Securities and Exchange Commission. But he dismissed it as it as a minor incident, Ms. Altschul recalls.

And Ms. Altschul said she was in no position to be picky. A successful toy inventor who had licensed toys like Bendy Wendy, a bendable doll, she had bet ''the farm'' on her cellphone ideas. On Nov. 8, 1999 -- the day The New York Times ran the first article on her cellphone patent, which helped attract subsequent media coverage and investors -- Ms. Altschul had maxed out 35 credit cards and owed a couple of hundred thousand dollars to family and friends.

According to her company's bankruptcy papers, the man she calls Dick in the book did line up some impressive investors: Harold Snyder, a generic drug manufacturer, invested $100,000, and Robert D. Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International, put in $75,000 of his own money.

But that was the extent of ''Dick's'' help, according to Ms. Altschul. She says he and his associates cost the company $200,000 -- in addition to $300,000 spent on worthless prototypes, tens of thousands squandered on questionable legal fees and $400 lunches -- and mishandled a funding deal with J. P. Morgan and a distribution deal with General Electric. This happened while Ms. Altschul was both trying to care for her mother, who was dying of a brain tumor, and watch over her father, who was suffering from dementia.

Could the financier be Dennis B. Levine, who, as a former broker at Drexel Burnham Lambert, spent two years in prison for feeding inside information to Ivan F. Boesky, the infamous arbitrager of the 1980's? Yes, according to people who have worked on the lawsuit against Ms. Altschul. The Dieceland bankruptcy filing lists a Dennis Levine as the company's financial consultant. Mr. Levine did not respond to phone messages.

Ms. Altschul said she would be unable to reveal any real names until current litigation is resolved, but even she admits that ''all you have to do is look at the public records to figure out who is who.''

Like most entrepreneurs, Ms. Altschul is highly resilient. She is already starting several new ventures, including beyondbitch.com, an Internet site where players rate the merits of other players' diatribes.

But her main money-making plan these days is her book. Ms. Altschul is hoping to earn enough from sales to pay back creditors and thus retain control of her phone patents.

Ms. Altschul's memoir (which, potential readers should be warned, could benefit from a vigorous editorial scrubbing) is printed by Kinko's and sold online for $19.95 plus shipping at www.assortedsnakes.com[http://www.assortedsnakes.com/]. She figures that all she has to do is sell 200,000 copies. Kristine Puopolo, a senior editor at Broadway Books, sees that as wildly ambitious: some books become best sellers having sold only one-tenth that number. But Ms. Altschul seems undeterred.

''So far I've already sold 1,000,'' Ms. Altschul says brightly. ''Only 199,000 to go.''

SE  Money and Business/Financial Desk; Section 3
HD  A Trader's Formula for Success: Give Back at Least 4 Percent
BY  By Landon Thomas Jr. (COMPILED BY MARK A. STEIN)
WC 268 words
PD 20 April 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 2, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

Arthur J. Pacheco, a senior Nasdaq trader at Bear, Stearns, may not have been picked for the top job at Nasdaq -- a post he openly lobbied for --but he will be honored next month for his contributions to the National Foundation for Teaching Entrepreneurship.

And he couldn't be happier: ''N.F.T.E. does a great job teaching poor kids self-esteem,'' Mr. Pacheco said by telephone from his desk on the Bear, Stearns trading floor. ''For a poor boy from the South Bronx, life has been pretty good to me.''

TD 

The foundation, based in New York, teaches entrepreneurial skills to low-income youths aged 11 to 18.

Its 10th annual dinner is to be held on May 19 at the Marriott Marquis hotel in Times Square, and John C. Bogle, the founder and former chairman of the Vanguard Group, will be honored along with Mr. Pacheco.

It is not surprising to find a Bear, Stearns executive being honored for doing good works. Alan C. Greenberg, known as Ace, the firm's irrepressible former chairman, made it clear long ago to his top managers that he expected them to hand over at least 4 percent of their gross income to charity.

That fact is well known in the nonprofit world. ''I get solicited pretty badly,'' Mr. Pacheco said, ''but I also give away more than my 4 percent, because they do check around here.'' Landon Thomas Jr.

SE  Money and Business/Financial Desk; Section 3
HD  A New Technology, Now That New Is Old
BY  By STEVE LOHR
WC 2478 words
PD 4 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 1, Column 2
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

MARTIN PICHINSON is one of Silicon Valley's undertakers. His company, Sherwood Partners, has carved out a prosperous niche as an expert in shutting down failed technology start-ups on behalf of venture capital firms and other disenchanted investors. And, this summer, he plans to move his company's headquarters to Palo Alto, Calif., the heartland of opportunity for his rapidly growing business.

Mr. Pichinson, 57, does not always bury companies. Some can be rehabilitated or sold off. But Sherwood Partners has shut down 150 once-aspiring start-ups in the last two years, and Mr. Pichinson figures that thousands more are destined to fold. His company stands ready to help things along.

TD 

''We're doctors of reality,'' said Mr. Pichinson, who began his business career in the garment industry. ''You don't have to have a Harvard M.B.A. to know that more money has to come in than go out.''

The winnowing of the corporate population is just one sign that the information technology industry is maturing in ways that will affect technology companies, their customers and investors for years to come. But what's painful for Silicon Valley is beneficial for those who use the stuff it produces.

The industry, according to Irving Wladawsky-Berger, a strategy executive at I.B.M., has entered ''the post-technology era.'' It is not that technology itself no longer matters, he explained. Instead, he said, the steady advances in chips, disk storage and software mean that the focus is no longer on the technology itself -- with its arcane language of processing speeds and gigabytes -- but on what people and companies can do with it.

As a result, industry executives and analysts say, the balance of power is shifting away from technology suppliers and toward their corporate customers. At the same time, the use of lower-cost building blocks of computer hardware and software is spreading, making it easier for companies and individuals to share data and work together using industry standards rather than remain dependent on one or two main suppliers.

These trends, they say, point to increased pressure on prices and profits for most technology companies, a good deal for corporate customers and a very tricky time for investors.

This is more than a backlash against the bubble years, or a mere pendulum swing in attitudes and practices. The technology itself will still deliver waves of innovation in the future. Yet an industry that has risen to account for 10 percent of the economy and nearly 60 percent of business capital spending can no longer play by its own rules.

''I don't see a loss of faith in technology, but gravity has been turned back on,'' said Dick Lampman, the director of Hewlett-Packard's research laboratories.

Yet an article published last week in The Harvard Business Review does question corporate America's faith in the value of technology. Titled ''IT Doesn't Matter,'' the article argues that information technology is inevitably headed in the same direction as the railroads, the telegraph, electricity and the internal combustion engine.

All of these industrial technologies aged from their boom-time youth to become, in economic terms, ordinary factors of production, or ''commodity inputs,'' the article noted. ''From a strategic standpoint, they became invisible; they no longer mattered,'' wrote Nicholas G. Carr, editor at large of The Harvard Business Review. ''That is exactly what is happening to information technology today.''

Most corporate executives, however, still think there is a lot they can do with technology to give them an edge. Glen Salow, the chief information officer of the American Express Company, sees the recent trends in the industry as working to his advantage. First, he said, the hard times in the technology business have increasingly meant that big corporate customers hold the upper hand in their dealings with suppliers. That shift, Mr. Salow added, has given him not only more bargaining power on price, but also more influence in the products and services that are developed.

Corporate customers want to use technology to achieve goals like cutting costs, improving customer service and speeding the pace of bringing new products to market. But in the past, the computer industry has often treated customers with a certain arrogance, selling raw technology, take it or leave it.

MR. SALOW recalled a conversation he had in the late 1990's with the chief executive of a major computer company, whom he declined to identify. As Mr. Salow recalled, the computer executive told him, ''My job is to make the hottest box on earth and deliver it on your loading dock.''

''You don't hear that kind of talk anymore from him or anyone else,'' he said. ''There has been a huge change from that perspective. The voice of the customer is being heard.''

Corporate customers are insisting that technology companies adopt industry-standard software formats and communications protocols, so that the many different kinds of computer systems in corporate data centers can work together. In doing so, the companies are encouraging a technology trend that was really kicked off by the Internet, with its global reach that connects all sorts of technologies. For the Internet to work, the computer plumbing had to cooperate. And hardware and software standards make it more difficult for technology companies to ''lock in'' customers to their more expensive, homegrown technology offerings.

With their new power, customers are also pressing for greater flexibility in how they buy computing resources -- especially as a utility-style service in which they pay only for as much as they use, as if they were buying electricity. Mr. Salow signed a utility pricing and outsourcing deal with I.B.M. last year, in which 1,500 technology workers were transferred to I.B.M. The arrangement, he explained, reduces fixed technology expenses, giving American Express more flexibility.

The widespread use of software standards, Mr. Salow added, enables the thousands of internal programmers at American Express to build new applications almost as if snapping together Lego blocks, reducing the amount of code that has to be written by hand. A result, he said, is that the software for, say, a new credit card offering or a fraud-detection feature can be built and put into use in about two weeks; five years ago, this might have taken six months.

''It all frees you up to take more gambles because each risk is not so costly and you can move a lot faster,'' Mr. Salow said.

Several big companies besides I.B.M. -- including Hewlett-Packard, Sun Microsystems, Microsoft, Oracle and Veritas -- are moving to utility computing. But Salesforce.com was founded in 1999 specifically to capitalize on the trend. It sells sales-force automation software over the Internet; users with a standard Web browser get access to it for a monthly fee.

Marc R. Benioff, the founder and chief executive of Salesforce.com, regards his company as a force for change in an industry that he says is still mired in the ''preutility era.'' In the future, Mr. Benioff said, ''the risk and complexity of computing should shift to the utility.''

Mr. Benioff's message and his low-cost, no-frills offering have gone over well at a time when companies are squeezing technology budgets.

Salesforce.com expects its revenue to about double this year, to $100 million. The company has started with sales-force automation, a software market where Siebel Systems is the leader.

Yet Mr. Benioff has ambitious plans to move into other big software markets where companies like SAP, PeopleSoft and others sell software for managing manufacturing, planning, human resources and other operations for large corporations. These big, complex ''enterprise'' software systems can cost millions of dollars and take several months or years to install and use.

''I think there is going to be a huge democratization of these enterprise technologies because of this on-demand computing enabled by the Internet,'' Mr. Benioff said. ''Our evangelical mission is to destroy enterprise software as it exists today.''

THE push toward utility computing, according to Mr. Wladawsky-Berger of I.B.M., fits neatly into his concept of a post-technology era. ''In the last few years,'' he said, ''the underlying components have become so powerful, reliable and inexpensive that you don't have to worry so much about the underlying engine, and you can move up to higher-level concerns.''

I.B.M. has moved more and more toward becoming a provider not only of technology, but also of business expertise in 17 industries from banking to electronics and transportation. Its $3.5 billion purchase of PricewaterhouseCoopers Consulting last year was a step toward that goal.

Mr. Wladawsky-Berger is in charge of guiding that strategy, which I.B.M. is promoting as ''on-demand computing.'' The on-demand concept represents a maturing of computing in a sense, he said, but more as ''the next evolutionary step in information technology, where the fun really begins'' rather than an aging industry in decline.

Each successive wave of computing -- from mainframes to minicomputers to personal computers to the Internet -- has opened the door to new users and created new problems. Each of those, in turn, must be addressed if the industry is to move ahead. The Internet brought an explosion of computing complexity. And while many dot-coms are gone, Internet technology has spread widely, becoming a mainstream technology in corporations.

Marc Andreessen, a co-founder of Netscape Communications, whose software introduced Web browsing to millions and touched off the Internet boom, is now chairman of Opsware, whose data-center software is intended to tackle the complexity crisis. ''At Netscape, we were building all the software components that made this possible and created the problem, and we didn't grasp the implications,'' said Mr. Andreessen, who is 31.

I.B.M., Hewlett-Packard, Microsoft and Sun have also begun major efforts to address the complexity problem with automated management tools that can replace people. But analysts say there is also room for innovative start-ups like Opsware.

''At the same time there is this relentless cost-cutting, there is also a lot of excitement about this market for tools to cope with the complexity conundrum, where a boatload of computer science is going to have to move into the corporate mainstream,'' said Mark Stahlman, an independent industry analyst.

Roger McNamee has been a professional investor in technology companies for more than two decades, starting as an analyst at T. Rowe Price. In 1991, backed by Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, the venture capital firm, Mr. McNamee became a co-founder of Integral Capital Partners, a so-called crossover fund investing in start-ups as well as fast-growing public companies. Integral Capital thrived in the 1990's. But late in the decade, Mr. McNamee said he and his partners became ''really concerned about how wacky the market had become.'' In the second half of 1999, at the peak of the bubble, Integral returned $1.5 billion to investors in its main fund.

In 1999, Mr. McNamee and three colleagues founded Silver Lake Partners to invest in mature companies. Silver Lake quickly raised $2.3 billion from institutional investors and wealthy individuals, including three of technology's best-known billionaires, Bill Gates, Lawrence J. Ellison and Michael Dell. Silver Lake's strategy is to make longer-term investments, taking a five-year perspective, of $250 million or so in each company. ''We're looking mostly for winners in consolidating industries,'' Mr. McNamee explained. ''This is a period of thinning the herd.''

And once the downturn is over, Mr. McNamee is skeptical that spending on technology will ever return to its long-term trend, dating back to the 1960's, of increasing at two to three times the growth rate of the economy. So, he said, broad-based technology funds are probably a formula for losing money. ''This is absolutely a stock-picker's environment,'' Mr. McNamee observed.

For its part, Silver Lake is betting that the corporate winners will include Flextronics, a contract manufacturer of computers, cellphones and other products; Seagate Technology, a disk drive maker; Gartner Inc., the technology research firm; and Ameritrade, the online broker.

One of Silver Lake's investors, Mr. Ellison, the chairman of Oracle, has been one of the most vocal proponents of the view that the technology industry is graying. ''Thousands of companies are on life support that just have to die,'' he said. ''Our industry is in the inevitable process of maturing.''

Yet Mr. Ellison's concept of a maturing industry is not exactly a listless old age. There will be fewer companies and slower growth, he said, but still plenty of leeway for entrepreneurial creativity. ''There will continue to be very cool new computing technologies,'' Mr. Ellison said.

Unlike so many industrial technologies -- railroads, say, or the telegraph -- the stored-program computer is a general-purpose tool, animated by software, a medium without material constraints. The unrelenting pace of improvement in processing speeds, data storage and miniaturization means the tools are more powerful and smaller; people then figure out things to do with them.

Indeed, innovation continues apace, despite the downturn. Advances are evident in a range of technologies -- wireless, data center automation, speech recognition, intelligent software, telephone service over the Internet, sensors, natural language processing, and on and on.

Underestimating the potential for computing has proved a common pitfall over the years, from Thomas J. Watson at I.B.M. in 1943 (''I think there is a world market for maybe five computers'') Ken Olsen at Digital Equipment in 1977 (''There is no reason anyone would want a computer in their home'').

Jim Gray, a computer scientist, has worked in the industry for more than 30 years. For his pioneering research on databases and transaction processing at I.B.M. and elsewhere, he won the 1999 A. M. Turing Award, sometimes called the Nobel of computer science. ''I've seen the 'end' at least twice in my career -- only to be surprised by the next wave,'' said Mr. Gray, who now works for Microsoft. ''My guess is that this computer thing has just gotten started.''

SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  A Large Pain: Health Coverage
BY  By JOSEPH B. TREASTER
WC 468 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 4, Column 5
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

IN corporate America, health insurance is so important in recruiting and retaining employees that nearly every big company provides it. But among small businesses, about 40 percent of owners say they simply cannot afford the coverage. And many that provide it have been cutting back and shifting costs to their workers. A result is that more than half of the 41.2 million uninsured Americans are employees of small businesses.

The main issue is price: costs have been rising an average of 10 to 40 percent for several years. Small businesses have experienced the most significant annual increases; by contrast, big companies have had increases of about 10 percent.

TD 

For many small businesses, the bill for health insurance often runs two or three times more than all other insurance costs combined. Summit Tile and Marble, a company in Woodinville, Wash., that sells and installs tile and marble, is paying $34,000 this year for health insurance for its 10 employees, compared with $10,000 for liability coverage for its equipment, cars and trucks. Total Media, a video and audiotape distributor in South Hackensack, N.J., is paying nearly $58,000 for health insurance for its 15 employees this year compared with $27,000 for all other insurance combined.

''The rising costs are on everybody's minds,'' said John Nelson, a vice president at Warner Pacific Insurance Services in Westlake Village, Calif.

Small businesses have been hit with the greatest increases, partly because they create higher administrative costs for insurers and because claims costs can be spread more effectively over large groups.

Joseph Trapani, the president of Dascit/White & Winston, a wholesale broker of health insurance in New York, said small businesses were moderating the increases by turning to policies with features like higher deductible amounts and co-payments up to $50 for visits to doctors. Aetna and several other big insurers have begun offering slimmed-down packages to small businesses that promise considerable savings while maintaining comprehensive coverage.

Some businesses have found they can save considerably by shopping around. Michael Sollitto, the owner of the V & L Machine and Tool Company, a small machine shop in Clifton, N.J., said he cut his costs by several thousand dollars a year by shifting from Horizon Blue Cross Blue Shield of New Jersey to Guardian, the lowest of three bidders.

One solution has been to drop coverage. But rather than do that, Mr. Nelson of Warner Pacific said that many of his customers in California had been moving to less expensive plans. They have often overlooked the cheapest plan, he said, for something in the middle range. ''They don't want to adjust their benefits too drastically,'' he said, ''for fear of losing their employees.''

CLM GETTING STARTED
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  Big Mistakes! And Some Tips on How to Avoid Them
BY  By JULIE CONNELLY
WC 1591 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 6, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

THIS may seem like the worst of times to start a small business, but in some ways, a downturn is the best of times.

''Bad times make you more focused,'' said Judith Gerberg, a career counselor in New York who started her own business 18 years ago. ''You are going to be more conscious of what you are spending on. You'll be slower to expand and more cautious about hiring. Your expectations will be more reasonable.''

TD 

A ready supply of talent, an abundance of office space and less competition for financing are other reasons to consider starting a business when the economy is lagging, experts say. But regardless of the economic climate, there are rules that apply to anyone embarking on an entrepreneurial venture, at any time.

Of every 100 Americans 18 years or over, 6.2 are engaged in starting a business, according to a study funded by the Ewing Marion Kauffman Foundation in Kansas City, Mo., which provides education and information about small businesses. Unfortunately, new business failures are common. ''Most new businesses fail, and they do so within the first three years,'' said Rebecca Macieira-Kaufmann, an executive vice president who specializes in small business at Wells Fargo, the banking company.

Educate Yourself

Plug the words ''starting your own business'' into any search engine and you will be overwhelmed by the number of responses. Among them is My Own Business (www.myownbusiness.org[http://www.myownbusiness.org/]), a Web site operated by a nonprofit group that offers a free online course on starting a business. There is also FastTrac (www.fasttrac.org[http://www.fasttrac.org/]), which offers classes in small business around the country. Rob Chernow, the senior vice president for entrepreneurship at the Ewing Marion Kauffman Foundation, which sponsors FastTrac, said: ''The courses help people figure out if being an entrepreneur is for them. They are able to flesh out if they are likely to have a viable business.''

Consider Moonlighting

''Who says we are obliged to quit our jobs when we start a business?'' said Philip Holland, the author of ''How to Start a Business Without Quitting Your Job.'' ''I once did but only because at the time I didn't know any better.''

Assuming you do not have an enormous need for sleep, keeping your day job gives you income and something to fall back on if the new venture fails. If you are thinking of starting a business in a field that you are unfamiliar with, consider getting a second job in that field before taking the plunge. After you have been on your feet all day as a chambermaid-cum-waitress at a charming little bed-and-breakfast, you may not fantasize so much about opening one. You will also have a better idea of what is required.

Harness Your Passion

Passion underlies the sweat equity that you need to start your own business. Amy Millman, the president of Springboard Enterprises, a Washington company that helps women entrepreneurs find investors, said, ''Entrepreneurs underestimate the importance of passion to get them through the hard times.''

Then there is the cash commitment that lenders or investors usually want to see before they agree to back your business. Even the Small Business Administration, a federal agency that guarantees bank loans to small businesses, expects you to have equity in your business totaling 25 to 50 percent of the loan you are requesting.

Carolyn A. Minerich of Jacksonville, Ala., invested both sweat equity and cash in her business. She took the $250,000 in savings she and her husband had accumulated and in 1996 started Carmin Industries, a metal-fabricating company that uses a water-jet technology that can cut through as much as six inches of stone and metal without burning the edges. It took years for the company to get off the ground, but today Ms. Minerich has a business earning annual revenues of $1 million, a 30,000-square-foot factory, 20 employees and clients including Walt Disney World in Florida, which ordered a 50-foot Mickey Mouse hand.

Raj Khera of Rockville, Md., started GovCon.com in 1995 with his brother Vivek and another partner. The Internet portal enables contractors to learn about bidding opportunities for government projects. Mr. Khera and his partners were software consultants by day and worked on their business at night.

Four years later, GovCon was sold to VerticalNet, and Mr. Khera and his brother used their profits to start MailerMailer .com, a company that helps businesses organize and maintain e-mail lists.

Develop a Thick Skin

Get used to rejection because you will experience a lot of it. ''I'm constitutionally unable to hear the word no,'' said Lorraine C. Scarpa, a co-founder of ImmediateFX, a software firm in Monroe, Conn.

Stay Focused

Focus is the process of defining your business -- what your niche is, who your customers are and how you will serve them. ''You need to find out what you can make money at, what customers would buy from you,'' Mr. Khera said. ''You'll always have competitors, so is there something you can make that fits into a specific niche?'' Also, when you are looking for financing, investors and lenders will want to know what makes you different from the others out there and how well you understand your market.

Before starting ImmediateFX with James Friedman and another partner, Ms. Scarpa and Mr. Friedman used to meet for lunch to talk about problems in the marketing industry -- she from the perspective of a buyer of marketing data, he as the head of a company that provided it.

''We had that 'Aha!' moment in a restaurant,'' Mr. Friedman recalled. ''We realized we had a business.'' In 1999, they started their company, selling software to integrate marketing data, and now have a business with annual revenues of $5 million.

Keep It Simple

It is one thing to be flexible when you are starting a business; and another to lose your focus by taking on any -- and all -- business to bring in money. This was a temptation faced by Michael Kimelman and Sheila Baird when they started their investment advisory firm, Kimelman & Baird, in Manhattan in 1966. They began by focusing on undervalued securities and, despite pressure over the years to take a different approach, have remained that way. Now, they manage $300 million in assets.

Besides sticking to their core business, Ms. Baird said, they kept a strict eye on costs. She advised anybody starting a company to ''spend your money on systems and personnel that you need to run the business, but don't let yourself get saddled with heavy costs because then you are tempted to do things away from your focus just to cover your costs.''

Although they paid themselves salaries, the partners did not give themselves raises for the first 15 years. If there were profits at the end of the year, the partners shared them, ''but we never increased our salaries,'' Mr. Kimelman said. ''That would have meant more overhead.''

Putting Success on Paper

''People tend just to dive in without thinking where they hope to get with their business,'' said Emily Swenson, a partner at Gansevoort Media, a financial consulting firm in New York.

Ms. Swenson said that entrepreneurs must always be able to answer questions like, How much money do you want to be making in three years? How many employees will you have? How will you be making your product? Where will you be selling it?

Having the answers in your head is not enough; you must also write them down in the form of a business plan. ''All business owners should have a business plan,'' said Ms. Macieira-Kaufmann of Wells Fargo. ''It is very hard to have a dialogue about financing without one.''

The thought of writing a business plan strikes fear in the hearts of many would-be entrepreneurs. It should not, though, because there is so much help available. For example, the book ''Business Plans That Work,'' by Joan Gillman, consists of strategies that entrepreneurs have used successfully to raise money. Another useful and comprehensive guide can be found on the Small Business Administration's Web site (www.sba.gov[http://www.sba.gov/]). There is an outline that you can customize for your business, with thorough explanations of what should be included in each section.

''My advice is, don't make it too lengthy -- keep it simple,'' said Bonita Walsh, the senior managing director for the commercial markets group at Citibank. ''You want to describe the business, how you are going to get customers, what the competition is and how the money will come in and go out.''

Cash Is King

When small businesses fail, it is usually because of cash-flow problems. ''There are very few businesses where the cash comes in equally throughout the year,'' Ms. Gillman said. As most entrepreneurs discover, customers do not always pay their bills on time. But business owners, unfortunately, have expenses like salaries and rent that have to be paid on a regular basis.

Managing cash flow is all about collecting from customers in a timely way without alienating them, and creating a timetable for paying suppliers without irritating them. Profit and cash are not the same thing, and a business that can show accounting profits can still fold.

Remember the line, ''Show me the money!,'' from the movie ''Jerry Maguire''? Consider that no sale is real until you see the green.

CLM BIG BUSINESS
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  When Goliath Comes Knocking on David's Door
BY  By BARNABY J. FEDER
WC 1545 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 13, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

WHEN the world's biggest companies do business with the millions of minnows on the commercial landscape, the outcome can be tough for the little guys.

Small companies routinely sustain losses struggling to get -- and keep -- the attention of giants that they hope to supply with products or services. But those who operate small businesses say that buying from a behemoth can also lead to pain.

TD 

''For them, it's one of a million things,'' said Barry Weinbaum, president and chief executive of NanoOpto, a start-up vendor of optical components for communications gear, based in Somerset, N.J. ''For us, it's life or death.''

Such thinking led Mr. Weinbaum, a former Lucent Technologies executive who knows big companies from the inside, to choose the Innovative Computing and Networking Corporation, a reseller of information systems, over dealing directly with I.B.M. when NanoOpto set up its computer system. ''I like to know I am talking to the president if there is any problem,'' Mr. Weinbaum said.

Still, for I.B.M. and numerous other corporate giants, selling to small businesses is a growth area, and they are trying harder than ever to court them. American Express has been steadily adding new features to its credit card for small business. ''We created the special plastic for small business category in 1996,'' said Kerry Hatch, an executive vice president at American Express, ''and it's been heating up ever since.''

When it comes to finance, American Express most likely has the broadest relationship with small businesses. In addition to its credit-card business, which some small companies use to cover hundreds of thousands of dollars a month in cash needs, American Express has been expanding its leasing operations for equipment that costs $250,000 or less. Last year, it created a small-business network called OPEN (www.openamericanexpress.com[http://www.openamericanexpress.com/]) to bring together various services, Web tools and discount programs with other giants like Exxon Mobil, Dell Computer, FedEx and Staples, allowing customers to save money on common expenses.

OPEN also aims to encourage small businesses to do much of their record keeping on its Web site. This month, it will start updating quarterly statements weekly online and make it easier for customers to categorize expenses differently. ''Limousine drivers might want to track gasoline expenses differently than a construction firm,'' said Ms. Hatch, who is also the general manager of OPEN. ''We want to make the Internet the best way of doing business with us going forward.''

Many of these large companies with products to sell to small businesses rely primarily on resellers, like Innovative Computing (which handles I.B.M. products and many others), and on the Internet. But many have not given up on more direct contacts.

I.B.M. tracks venture capital announcements and calls on new companies that have just raised cash. Unlike most small businesses, start-ups are often in the market for more than a single product. And when I.B.M. comes across a venture like Pristine.com, a company in White Plains, N.Y., that trains stock traders, it may offer more than its usual line of products, services and flexible financing, said Laura Avolio, an I.B.M. account representative. I.B.M. has sponsored some of Pristine.com's seminars and offered free use of I.B.M. meeting rooms in return for the chance to show its trading equipment to Pristine's students.

I.B.M. is also trying to individualize its treatment of small businesses at IBM.com, its Web site, at least when it comes to selling. Its small and medium-size business portal (www-1.ibm.com /businesscenter/us) has call-me or text-chat buttons that are connected to products.

The company has begun trolling for more small-business customers through an auction program on eBay, selling equipment that I.B.M. has refurbished or recently phased out. About 80 percent of the equipment is sold to small businesses that are new to I.B.M. and half have agreed to receive calls from the company with other offers, according to Alex G. Gogh, director of IBM.com marketing.

At Microsoft, a business-software group has developed a more imaginative approach to serving the small-business market by focusing on Southridge, an executive search firm in Buffalo. Product engineers and marketing experts in the Microsoft contingent want to know how they can convince Maggie Simmons, Southridge's owner, that Microsoft products and services will increase her profits. They also try to help Sean O'Bryan, her office manager and accountant, keep the server, which supports the office's desktop computers, and printers humming, and to make it easy for Sally Taylor, a clerical worker, to use Microsoft software for tasks like managing customer lists.

Most of all, Microsoft aims to impress Stuart Walker, Southridge's technology consultant and troubleshooter. To sell to Southridge, Microsoft needs consultants like Mr. Walker to be up to date on every new wrinkle it introduces into its small-business-product lineup.

The one thing that Microsoft, the world's largest software company, does not expect from Southridge is feedback. The search firm and its employees are pure fiction, a small business and its key figures conjured up by researchers to help Microsoft maintain a detailed image of real small-business customers as it tries to increase its sales to them. To enhance the fiction, Microsoft has printed and distributed trading cards for its employees picturing Maggie and the others and, on the back, describing their jobs and characteristics.

''We often ask, 'What would Stuart do?' '' said Katy Hunter, group product manager for Windows Server, the Microsoft operating system for computers that acts as a hub for small networks of desktop computers and other devices.

THE cards are one element in a broad Microsoft drive to increase sales to small and midsize businesses, to $10 billion by the end of the decade from $300 million this year.

One tendency these and other giants have to fight, marketing experts said, is the practice of grouping small- and midsize businesses together. Definitions vary widely, with estimates of the number of small businesses in the United States ranging from 10 million to 20 million, accounting for $150 billion or more in annual purchases.

''Small businesses don't like to be lumped into one giant pool based on size, regardless of segment, age and needs,'' said Ken Lubar, a managing director at EMI Strategic Marketing in Boston. ''There's a big gap between $1 million in revenue and $50 million.''

The larger small businesses are more likely to have multiple locations and specialized executives, like a controller, a full-time marketing manager or someone overseeing manufacturing. ''The wife isn't doing the bookkeeping and there is more reliance on professionals to make decisions,'' Mr. Lubar said.

If there is one thing smaller businesses have in common, Mr. Lubar said, it is the little long-term planning they do, an aspect that large organizations have trouble with, especially when these customers might be ready to buy services. ''They are I-need-it-now decision makers,'' he said.

But the AT&T Corporation, which serves about 3.9 million businesses with fewer than 100 employees, said that a common denominator seemed to be a desire for simplicity, like a single bill for all communications services or one number to call for service problems. AT&T has also found that simplicity is important when it is selling small companies the technology products that midsize businesses buy. It bundles such data management, networking and other abilities into convenient packages, said Mark Metzendorf, vice president in charge of AT&T's small and midsize business unit.

Big businesses think that sophisticated but straightforward Internet tools are an important selling point for many small businesses. America Online, a unit of AOL Time Warner, introduced AOL for Small Business last fall after surveys showed that many small-business subscribers to AOL were concerned that they did not take full advantage of the Internet. The site offers, among other things, tips on subjects like finance, how to get free publicity and how to use AOL to network with other businesses or potential customers.

Others are finding that though the Internet is efficient, it still cannot match the benefit of direct contact. About 40 percent of the orders last year on the main Web site of Grainger, the nation's largest distributor of machinery components, industrial hardware and maintenance supplies, came from businesses employing 10 or fewer people. But many of Grainger's most enthusiastic small-business customers, like Tomas Estrada, who owns Family Fences, a new fence-building company in Evanston, Ill., still use the Web only to identify what they need and to compare prices.

''Then I go to their store in Morton Grove,'' Mr. Estrada said. ''They are flexible on price and everybody there is very friendly, probably because I've been spending a lot of money.''

CLM WASHINGTON
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  The Little Guys Are Ready to Rumble
BY  By EDMUND L. ANDREWS
WC 1446 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 1, Column 4
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

WASHINGTON -- ANYONE who doubts that small business has big political clout need only recall the truncated two-year career of Senator Jean Carnahan.

A centrist Democrat from Missouri, Mrs. Carnahan was appointed to the Senate in 2000 after her husband, Mel, the governor of the state, was killed during his senate race but won the election posthumously. Within months, Mrs. Carnahan was bombarded by demands from small-business owners to join Republicans on a vote to permanently repeal the estate tax.

TD 

The letters came by the hundreds -- passionate, personalized and sometimes even handwritten on company invoices. ''I'm going to be 74 soon and my two sons will get what I have when I die,'' wrote the owner of a printing company. ''They should not have to pay taxes of 55 percent on the small business they own.''

When Mrs. Carnahan voted against the measure, she became a target for the National Federation of Independent Business, a nationwide lobbying group of 600,000 small businesses and a machine to mobilize them. ''She is sucking the lifeblood out of your business,'' the federation charged in a flier picturing a hypodermic needle stuffed with money. Placards extolled her Republican challenger for the Senate in 2002, Representative James M. Talent, as a ''proven friend'' of small business.

Political organizers for the lobbying group in Washington attracted hundreds of thousands of dollars in campaign contributions for Mr. Talent, while members in Missouri organized events to promote him and attack Mrs. Carnahan, who had begun the campaign well ahead in the polls but saw her lead evaporate to go down in defeat by 22,000 votes. National Republican groups were also mobilizing to defeat Mrs. Carnahan. But political analysts say the small-business federation played a crucial role, as it did in more than 30 other Congressional races in which it was active.

''Small business has gone from being a player to being the dominant player in the business community,'' said Dirk Van Dongen, president of the National Association of Wholesaler-Distributors and a longtime lobbyist in Washington.

''The big-business guys are more pragmatic, giving a 60-40 split in money to the two parties,'' he said. ''The small-business guys are much more ideological.''

As a bitterly divided Congress moves into the decisive phase of battles over President Bush's $726 billion tax-cutting plan, the small-business political machinery is again playing a major role. When the president began a campaign last month on behalf of his plan, he invited more than 250 small-business owners and lobbyists to show their support in a Rose Garden ceremony. When Congress convened for spring break, small-business advocates were waiting in states like Maine and Ohio to influence lawmakers through advertising, meetings and organized events. And the federation is not alone in its activism. Many big lobbying groups that represent small companies pledged their support for Mr. Bush's plan before they knew what was in it. They did so partly on the conviction that any package of tax cuts would be good, but also on the expectation that support for the president's top priorities would mean far more support for their own agenda.

Many admit they were surprised that the biggest element of the plan was the president's proposal to eliminate taxes on stock dividends, a proposal that has no direct impact on most small companies, which are overwhelmingly privately held and do not pay dividends.

''I've been in tax lobbying for 20 years now, and I never spent more than five minutes thinking about dividend taxes,'' said Mr. Van Dongen.

In justifying their no-questions-asked support for the Bush plan, including the dividend tax cut, small-business lobbyists say that other features will provide a huge benefit to their clients.

Mr. Bush's proposal to accelerate the reduction of individual tax rates, they say, will affect hundreds of thousands of small companies that report their profits through the individual tax returns of their owners. That population includes sole proprietorships, from family grocery stores to local dry cleaners, as well as hundreds of thousands of so-called S corporations. ''About 85 percent of our members pay the bulk of their taxes on their individual returns,'' said Daniel Danner, senior vice president responsible for federal programs at the National Federation of Independent Business. ''Their personal return and their business return is one and the same.''

The other major enticement for small businesses is a seemingly mundane proposal to triple the amount of money spent on new equipment that a small business can write off as an immediate expense against profits. The proposal, which is of huge interest to mom-and-pop companies that are buying, say, computers or cars or dry-cleaning equipment, would raise the amount to $75,000.

Beyond the immediate issue, however, groups like the National Federation of Independent Business have cultivated their reputations as movers and shakers with easy access to the top echelons of the White House and Congress. One such player is Jack Faris, president and chief executive of the National Federation of Independent Business. ''You can rest assured that Andy Card will return his phone calls,'' said one veteran lobbyist, referring to Andrew H. Card Jr., President Bush's chief of staff.

Democrats, of course, try to woo the small-business vote. John Kerry, the Democratic senator from Massachusetts who is running for president, unveiled a plan last month for tax cuts specifically aimed at small companies. But the Democrats face an uphill fight. There is no question that Republican leaders are more closely aligned with the agenda being pushed by small-business lobbies, and that they listen closely to small-business complaints.

For instance, small-business groups were enraged by regulations imposed in the waning days of the Clinton administration that protected workers against physical problems like repetitive-stress injury from computer keyboards. They amassed a huge coalition that ranged from restaurateurs to courier services, all arguing that the so-called ergonomic requirements would impose major costs and risks to small companies.

Largely by invoking the mantra ''small business,'' a phrase with vague but deeply sympathetic resonance among voters, the Bush administration and Congressional Republicans brushed off intense opposition by labor unions and rescinded most of the regulations.

''What small businesses want most of all is to be left alone,'' said Frank C. Fillmore Jr., founder of the Fillmore Group, a technology consulting firm in Ellicott City, Md., that has five employees.

That mantra dovetails with the Bush administration's agenda. But the two agendas are not the same, and some veteran lobbyists worry that small-business groups run the risk of being shortchanged.

The most immediate concern is about the tax plan. When the Senate adopted a budget resolution last month that limited the tax cut to $350 billion over 10 years, compared with $550 billion embraced by the House, it greatly increased the probability that part of Mr. Bush's plan would be scrapped.

The question is, which part? President Bush and his top economic team are passionately in favor of the dividend tax cut, which would cost more than $350 billion over 10 years. Small-business groups, on the other hand, are adamant about the expensing provisions and the individual rate reductions.

John S. Satagaj, president of the Small Business Legislative Council, an organization of small trade associations, said the support of small business groups for the entire plan and their appearance in the Rose Garden could undermine their own priorities. ''Where our influence is greatest is in the perception that this is what small business wants,'' Mr. Satagaj said. ''When they are in the Rose Garden and the dividend issue is front and center, the message gets out that this is what small business wants.''

Small-business lobbyists have been jolted before. After campaigning for Mr. Bush's tax-cutting package in 2001, the groups were stunned when their beloved expensing provision was dropped during Congressional negotiations.

Mr. Van Dongen, who organized the Tax Relief Coalition, a lobbying group of 1,000 companies and others, before Mr. Bush even announced his newest plan, said the risk was worth taking. ''There are no guarantees in this game,'' he said. ''But if that fact causes you to pull your punches because you're afraid of losing, you have in effect negotiated yourself out of the game.''
