CLM TECHNOLOGY
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  On the Web, Without Wasting Time
BY  By LISA GUERNSEY
WC 1602 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 10, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

WHEN Alan Lethers, a small-business owner in San Luis Obispo, Calif., thinks about the amount of time, money and energy that can be spent on a Web site, he is reminded of an old saying, ''You're so busy fighting the alligators that you forget you're supposed to be draining the swamp.''

At Mr. Lethers's company, which is called Patio Pacific, America's Finest Pet Doors (petdoors.com), he and his nine employees must first look after receiving supplies, taking orders and manufacturing and shipping its products. Working on the Web site is another task to be squeezed in. ''We little guys have a hard time devoting the resources we need to do it,'' Mr. Lethers said.

TD 

Still, he said, having a Web site is worth the trouble. Sales of his pet doors -- the kind that fit into sliding glass doors -- have increased twofold since he went online.

The Kelsey Group, a research company in Boston, recently found that the number of small businesses (defined as 100 employees or less) with a Web site has increased, but not as fast as had been predicted. In a 2002 survey of businesses that advertise, 43 percent had a Web site, up from 36 percent in 1999.

Consider your local hair salon, said Neal Polachek, a senior vice president and small-business specialist at the Kelsey Group. Maybe they are getting all the customers they need through old-fashioned word of mouth. For businesses like that, he said, ''having a Web site is not necessarily the be-all to end-all.''

By contrast, many small businesses that seek customers nationally have found that a Web site can be a useful sales channel or marketing tool. A modest but healthy subset of small businesses has even prospered on the Web alone.

Small businesses that want to build a Web site will find that doing so is easier than in the pioneering mid-90's. New software and services have sprung up that bypass many of the hassles in setting up a site or a Web store. The intent is to allow business owners to drain the swamp instead of fight the alligators.

Here are examples of how new tools and services are helping many types of businesses:

Web Site Templates

''Setting up a Web site used to be an exercise in frustration,'' said Mie-Yun Lee, the founder of BuyerZone .com, a site that matches small businesses with technology companies that sell the equipment they need. People had to learn HTML code, or Hypertext Markup Language; abbreviations like F.T.P. (file transfer protocol); and strange uses of familiar words like ''socket'' (a method of communicating with network programs). ''They would say, F.T.P.? What is F.T.P.?'' Ms. Lee said.

Today's e-commerce services have enabled entrepreneurs to set up Web sites by picking a template, selecting colors and themes and inserting texts of their choice. No coding is required, and, depending on the Web-building service, no knowledge of F.T.P. is necessary. Additions or substitutions to a page are simply done with the click of a button on a Web site.

Adam Noble is a college student in Florida who started a Web business in 2000 as a broker selling parts for customizing cars, like specialty muffler tips. He had heard about FreeMerchant.com from a neighbor and used the service to set up his own site, calledAIEOnline.com, with a template. He pays $25 a month for the software, which is sold by Digital River, an e-commerce company. ''It took no more than a week,'' said Mr. Noble, who runs the business from his bedroom.

If you do not want to be hemmed in by a template and still want to avoid HTML, there is FrontPage, a program included in Microsoft Office software that many small-business owners already have. DreamWeaver, $399 software from Macromedia, offers more flexibility and creativity and is used by professional Web site developers. In both cases, you do not need to know any code.

Online Shopping Carts

Lynne Bingham started her business on the Web in 1994 as www .thestorkdelivers.com, creating custom baskets of baby gifts. ''We were the e-commerce guinea pig of Pittsburgh,'' she said. She is embarrassed to recall what her site looked like. ''Oh, God, it was a joke,'' she said.

In January 2001, she decided to revamp the site. She wanted to use what is called shopping cart software, which enables people to buy items online using a credit card. But she did not want to learn new computer code or pay the exorbitant prices that she first discovered for software of that type. (One vendor, she said, offered her software for $35,000.) She decided to try Yahoo Stores, a service that combines Web services like shopping cart software with marketing through Yahoo's shopping channel. It costs $50 a month, with fees for transactions, commissions and listings.

Now Ms. Bingham is listed as a top seller on Yahoo and has expanded to five employees.

Yahoo is not the only source for shopping cart software. (Several merchants, by the way, have grumbled that Yahoo Shopping's latest pricing is too expensive because it takes commissions from purchases.) Ralph F. Wilson, the author of several books on e-commerce, including an e-book on wilsonweb.com, which includes reviews of shopping cart products, describes several other shopping cart forms. Some of these include Storefront.com, ShopSite .com, freemerchant.com and 1ShoppingCart.com. While others are offered by BizLand, Homestead and Microsoft's bCentral. Prices range from $12 a month (plus fees) to a one-time charge of $1,300 to use the software.

Niche-Oriented Directories

Patrick Sutton, a kitchen designer in Alexandria, Va., started his Web site, suttondesign.com, in 1997. It became a showcase for his portfolio, but he was not sure how potential clients would find it. ''Back in '97, I put it up on a wing and a prayer without thinking of how I was connected to other sites,'' Mr. Sutton said.

Now he is fully aware of how people find him. He can see, for example, that much of the traffic to his site comes from online directories that list architects and designers, like HGTV.com and ThisOldHouse.com. ''Directory sites are very important to me and I keep them updated,'' Mr. Sutton said.

Mr. Sutton is not assuming, however, that people know how to find these directories. Luckily, search engines like Google have become better at pointing people to those directories.

But search technology is not the only thing that has helped. A new form of online advertising, sometimes called keyword advertising or sponsored links, is gaining currency. There are various forms -- the search engine Overture is best known for the technique -- but essentially keyword advertising enables a company to connect quickly with people searching for precisely what the company offers.

Pages from the business's Web site are highlighted by the search engine after a search is performed, and advertisements on the right-hand side of pages lead shoppers directly to the ''store.''

''The business can target customers who need niche products like dog booties without the wasteful expense of mass mailings,'' wrote Joanne H. Pratt in a 2002 report from the Small Business Administration. ''Instead, customers find them simply by typing in the words ''dog booties.''

Stephanie Loftus of LoftusDesigns .com, a custom-furniture store, started using Overture in October and Google Adwords -- another form of keyword advertising -- three months ago. The response was so great that she and her husband, John, decided to close their showroom in Jenks, Okla., and do all their transactions on the Web. ''Now we ship coast to coast,'' Ms. Loftus said.

Automating EBay

EBay is known not only as a space for collectors, but also as a place for small businesses to make a name for themselves on the Web without building their own sites. By managing hundreds of auctions a week, a seller can turn eBay into a full-time job.

In the last few years, eBay has taken note of the small businesses that are selling on its site and developed tools to make it easier for them to conduct many transactions (and, of course, to allow eBay to collect large amounts of transaction fees).

Adam Noble, the college student who founded AIEOnline.com, started on eBay. At first, the more items he auctioned, the more the work turned tedious. Now he uses Turbo Lister, a free eBay tool available to sellers. ''You can get 20 auctions on eBay in a matter of minutes, instead of spending hours entering each one by one,'' Mr. Noble said.

Many other software products are also available to eBay sellers to automate their work, said Marsha Collier, author of ''Starting an eBay Business for Dummies.'' Companies like AuctionWorks.com and Andale.com help heavy sellers keep up with the auctions that close, the items that need to be shipped, the photographs that must be uploaded and the customers with questions.

''When you are listing 100 times a day and people are paying you at that quick a rate, it really helps to have a service that sends you this information, so that you aren't fumbling around in e-mail trying to keep track,'' she said.

CLM TECHNOLOGY
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  A Once and Present Innovator, Still Pushing Buttons
BY  By STEVE LOHR
WC 1824 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 1, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

CONCORD, Mass. -- EARLY one morning last month, 14 small-business owners gathered around a table and, over coffee and muffins, spoke for the next two hours of their curiosity and qualms about taking the technological plunge into Web sites, company e-mail addresses and perhaps even selling over the Internet.

They ran genuinely small businesses, most with a handful of employees, some sole proprietors among them. They sold bridal clothes, antiques, jewelry, security systems, flowers, herbal-care products, greeting cards and gourmet foods. Most of them said they used e-mail-messages regularly, but none had company e-mail addresses or Web sites.

TD 

This focus-group session was one of dozens sponsored in the last few months by Interland, a company that is hoping to cash in on the potentially vast but tricky market for helping small businesses get online. Behind the one-way mirror at this session and others sat Dan Bricklin, Interland's chief technology officer. His job is to study how small businesses do, and do not, use technology -- the needs, wishes and fears of their owners. Then, he must try to design simple, affordable software tools to give them what they want.

Sitting behind the mirror and observing the group inside, Mr. Bricklin said that the real challenge was to figure out how to reach a portion of the 20 million businesses in the United States with 10 employees or fewer and ease their entry onto the Internet.

''This is the mass market for small business,'' he said. ''Cracking this market is the holy grail.''

A daunting challenge, but if anyone is up to the task it is Mr. Bricklin, who has spent most of his career as an entrepreneurial small-businessman, as well as a skilled technologist.

Mr. Bricklin has already played an important role in helping democratize technology as a pioneering innovator in the personal computer industry and says that he thinks the Internet is the next step in delivering powerful technology to individuals and small businesses. ''The beauty of the PC was that it leveled the playing field and let small businesses do so much more,'' Mr. Bricklin said. ''This technology is that same kind of tool.''

He and his friend and collaborator, Bob Frankston, created the electronic spreadsheet program called VisiCalc, which jump-started the personal-computer revolution. The big money in spreadsheets would be made by big companies -- first Lotus Development, which bought Mr. Bricklin's firm, and later Microsoft. Yet Mr. Bricklin showed the way in 1979 with the spreadsheet program, a practical tool for business that allowed anyone with a personal computer to do financial modeling and simulation previously available only to corporations with mainframe computers and corps of research analysts.

By instinct, philosophy and breeding, Mr. Bricklin explained, he is a small-businessman.

His father, Baruch, and his grandfather, Simon, managed the family printing business in Philadelphia. Mr. Bricklin went to the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, where he caught the computing bug in the days before there were such things as personal computers. He earned an M.B.A. at the Harvard Business School, where he came up with the idea for VisiCalc.

Since then, he has moved from one idea and venture to another, including Demo, a simulation software program designed for nonprogrammers, and Slate, which made software for pen-based computers before that market emerged with simple products like hand-held computers. Some of Mr. Bricklin's efforts have been successful and others not. He has made a good living, if not a killing.

He personifies what Eric von Hippel, a professor of business at the Sloan School of Management at M.I.T., has called the ''lead-user phenomenon,'' observing that so much of practical innovation comes from a small number of imaginative people who tinker with the latest technology.

Mr. Bricklin first demonstrated that trait as a child, building a shortwave radio, a stereo that the family played for years and a kitchen intercom that his mother, Ruth, used to summon him to meals from his bedroom upstairs.

These days, Mr. Bricklin is tinkering with new gadgetry. He carries a pocket-size digital camera, the latest cellphone equipped with organizer software and a tiny keyboard and a new notebook personal computer with a screen that can be used as a tablet for writing notes with a stylus.

But his real interest lies in trying to bridge the gap between geeky technology and ordinary people.

''My focus has been mainly on the regular user,'' Mr. Bricklin said. ''The challenge is to make it easy to use tools.''

The corporate life and the blandishments of big business never appealed to him much. ''I didn't want to be a cog in a big machine,'' said Mr. Bricklin, a bearded 51-year-old who was wearing blue jeans, a flannel shirt and running shoes. ''You have more responsibility in a small business, but you also have more freedom. I love the freedom, and I love the hands-on nature of it -- you meet the customers and you build the product.''

TODAY Mr. Bricklin works for a larger enterprise because his latest company, Trellix, based here, was acquired last December by Interland, which has annual revenue of $120 million. As part of a bigger company with deeper pockets, he has a better chance to take his Web-site-building software to a wider market. ''Right now, I am in a bigger business, but I am representing small business,'' he said. ''I reek of small business.''

Interland, which is based in Atlanta, is betting that the use of the Internet by small businesses will follow the same pattern as the personal computer industry did years ago. Until the early 90's, said Joel J. Kocher, a former senior executive at Dell Computer who is now the chief executive of Interland, PC's were still difficult for many people to use, so they shunned them. But the adoption of Microsoft's Windows operating system, with its point-and-click icons, and other improvements, opened the door to a much larger market.

A similar phenomenon, Interland hopes, is about to unfold in the market for operating Web sites. In this market, a Web site ''hosting'' supplier, like Interland, runs the big data centers where millions of Web pages reside. But a full-featured Web site for browsing and shopping can still cost thousands of dollars a year and require technical expertise that small businesses lack.

Interland intends to lower that barrier by offering Web site hosting to small businesses for as little as $23 a month.

The company's basic service, introduced in April, includes a Web site, a dot-com domain name and 30 e-mail-message accounts. It offers templates for about 200 designs, the ability to add and edit pictures and publish and update text without having to program in HTML, or Hypertext Markup Language, the technical lingua franca of Web sites. To handle online purchases or include Web logs, or blogs, costs extra.

Many companies are chasing what is expected to be a sizable market for managing Web sites and e-commerce for small businesses. Industry analysts predict that the market will double or triple over the next three or four years, with estimates ranging up to $9 billion a year. These projections are best taken with a handful of salt, but the market does seem promising.

Besides Interland, the suppliers of Web hosting services include Affinity, NTT Verio, Homestead, Yahoo, regional Bell telephone companies and others. Many companies, like Microsoft and Macromedia, offer specialized software tools for building Web sites.

Interland bought Trellix, Mr. Kocher said, because it built the most comprehensive, easy-to-use tools for helping small businesses develop an online presence, an expertise that Mr. Bricklin and his team had worked on as they sold their software to companies that offered hosting services to small businesses and individuals.

''For us, the secret sauce is Dan Bricklin and his team's deep knowledge of small business,'' Mr. Kocher said.

In the focus group, a woman who manages a bridal shop said she was concerned because customers asked if she has a Web site, and she has to tell them no.

''You hear that all the time in these sessions -- the customers are asking,'' Mr. Bricklin said behind the mirror. ''Having a Web site has become a generational necessity for a lot of businesses. You lose the people under 30 without it.''

Several of the small-business owners also expressed worries about the time, costs and technical hassles of going online. ''Small businesses are often very skeptical of new things,'' Mr. Bricklin said. ''That's logical because they have to make decisions quickly. They don't have the time to do research, or the staff to do it for them.''

So small businesses tend to adopt what Amar Bhide, a professor of business at the Columbia University Graduate School of Business, called a ''heads I win, tails I don't lose much'' attitude toward risk.

That approach, Mr. Bricklin said, was precisely why most small businesses should think of moving online.

Don't try to be Amazon.com with a full e-commerce presence all at once. Step in gradually, he said, by starting with a Web site and company e-mail. ''You have to try it out -- see what works for you and what doesn't work for you,'' he said.

The Internet, Mr. Bricklin said, is best viewed as an important but supplemental tool for business. ''It is a medium of business that you have to understand, just like doing business over the telephone or face-to-face selling are mediums of business,'' he said.

As a medium, the Internet enables even a small business to present a lot of information about itself and allows customers and suppliers to interact through a Web site or e-mail. Even if the information is as simple as store hours, it helps.''The person who wants to drop by your store on the way to work wants to know if you're going to be open at 8 a.m., and he wants to know that at 10:30 p.m. the night before,'' Mr. Bricklin said.

For most small businesses, he added, a reasonable goal for an online presence is to increase sales by 10 percent without having to add more employees.

''That is huge -- it means a better vacation,'' he said. ''Remember, for these companies the bottom line is their pocketbook.''

CLM TRENDS
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  Young Blacks Try Entrepreneurship
BY  By DANIEL ALTMAN
WC 1273 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 8, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

THERE is little official evidence to go by, but the anecdotes are pouring in: across the country, more young black professionals are stepping off the corporate ladder to become entrepreneurs. Joining a big company used to be perceived as the surest route to material success by many black professionals, but lately, owning a business has become as attractive, or more so.

Four years ago, Billye R. Hill was on the old track. With an M.B.A. from the Georgia Institute of Technology in hand, she joined the interactive marketing team at Procter & Gamble in Cincinnati. She loved her job, and thought she would stay for at least five years. But, she said, ''I knew going in the door I wasn't meant to work for someone.''

TD 

Soon, Ms. Hill founded a Web site design company as a sideline. As the economy began contracting, Procter & Gamble started offering buyouts to employees as part of a downsizing plan. Ms. Hill, who is now 27, saw an opportunity: she took a payment of about $25,000 -- enough to keep her afloat for several months -- and turned all her attention to her small business.

Ms. Hill's story is on its way to becoming commonplace in Cincinnati, said De Asa M. Brown, the chief executive of the Greater Cincinnati Northern Kentucky African American Chamber of Commerce. The area is home to a large black population and several Fortune 500 companies. Downsizing during the economic downturn, Ms. Brown said, gave people like Ms. Hill the impetus to strike out on their own.

''They had an opportunity to get a great buyout, which was the income they needed to launch their dream,'' she said.

In the past, the decision Ms. Hill made would have been more difficult, said Barbara B. Lang, the chief executive of the District of Columbia Chamber of Commerce. The new entrepreneurs are ''willing to take risks that 10 years ago, 20 years ago, African-Americans were not willing to take,'' she said. ''It's an absolute change in attitude, and not having to feel that the corporate structure is the only way -- or the safe way.''

One factor supporting that change is increasing education. Starting a business is ''just a natural extension of the career trajectory from getting a degree -- a law degree, an M.B.A., whatever,'' said Timothy Bates, a professor of urban and labor studies and economics at Wayne State University in Detroit, who has studied black entrepreneurship for more than three decades.

Education has also improved access to capital, long an obstacle for black entrepreneurs. Traditionally, they obtained financing from family, friends and local banks on the strength of their reputations, said Steven S. Rogers, a professor of entrepreneurship at Northwestern University in Evanston, Ill. But not anymore.

''What we're starting to see are more and more African-American entrepreneurs who have M.B.A.'s,'' Professor Rogers said. ''Now, you're seeing a new generation of African-American entrepreneurs who are looking to access capital from all the available resources.''

Professor Bates at Wayne State recently completed a study, with William D. Bradford, a professor of finance at the University of Washington, of venture capital firms that specialize in investing in minority-owned businesses. ''The minority venture capital industry has grown over time,'' he said. ''Probably in the 90's it increased about tenfold.''

The study, financed by the Ewing Marion Kauffman Foundation in Kansas City, Mo., which promotes education and entrepreneurship, started with venture capital investments made from 1989 to 1995 and tracked them through 2000. Just more than half the investments yielded a positive return, and most of the venture capital funds made money. The foundation will release the study in its entirety later this month.

In terms of rates of return, Professor Bates said, the minority-focused firms may be ''slightly outperforming'' the venture capital industry as a whole.

The census bureau also compiles statistics on minority-owned businesses, but it is just now preparing a survey for 2002. The latest figures, which compare 1997 to 1992, show that the number of black-owned companies rose by 26 percent in that period while businesses overall grew by only 7 percent. Looking more deeply at the data, Professor Bates said, reveals other promising trends.

''When you're looking solely at the top-end firms, you'll see that the growth rate is more than twice as high as it is for black businesses generally,'' he said. ''That's been going for a while.''

Between 1992 and 1997, the numbers of relatively small service-oriented businesses like barbershops and beauty parlors barely increased. ''The growing firms,'' Professor Bates said, ''are the types of firms that are selling to corporate customers, government customers -- the types of firms that are competing in the broader marketplace.''

And in his own study of companies receiving venture capital, he added, ''when you look at the profile of the owners, you're looking at people coming out of elite jobs in corporate America.''

In Washington, corporate America means big law and public relations firms, more so than Fortune 500 companies. ''A lot of lawyers either leave and go into a single-practitioner, or go into industries very different from their law training,'' said Ms. Lang of the District of Columbia Chamber of Commerce. ''The law degree is just a credential for them.''

Given the current state of the economy, it is not surprising that some of the new entrepreneurs left their old jobs involuntarily. But the dislocation can act as an impetus to strike out on one's own, Professor Rogers said, especially among people who are looking to guarantee their families' futures. ''There's a generation of people that are more into pursuing wealth creation now, rather than just saying, 'I was dissatisfied because of the glass ceiling.' ''

Nevertheless, the weak economy has made the road to small-business success more difficult for the new cohort of black entrepreneurs.

Michael L. Hines, 29, started a small marketing firm four years ago while working for Golin/Harris International, a public relations division of the Interpublic Group of Companies, the marketing and communications giant. ''I ended up making more money in that company than I did in my full time,'' he said.

Now, Mr. Hines employs five people at Onemarketspace.com, a market research firm in Washington. But his industry sustained some of the biggest losses outside of investment banking in the economic downturn of the last few years. ''It's obviously just a slow market,'' he said, where making money has become extremely difficult.

Despite the economic constraints, Mr. Hines views entrepreneurship among young black professionals as a robust trend. ''Most young African-American men I know are entrepreneurs,'' he said. ''Some are doing significantly well, but we all seem to be doing at least as well as our counterparts in corporate America, even now, even here.''

Ms. Lang said she still believed, however, that black entrepreneurs faced special obstacles: ''While access to capital is better than it used to be, there are still issues. The playing field has not been completely leveled. It's better than it was 10 years ago, but it's not where it needs to be.''

CLM INSURANCE
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  Finding Ways to Afford the Safety Net
BY  By JOSEPH B. TREASTER
WC 1466 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 4, Column 3
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

FOR years, the hassle of dealing with taxes has been the No. 1 problem for small businesses. But late last year the rising cost of insurance edged ahead as the biggest headache, ranked in national surveys before taxes, the sluggish economy and poor sales.

Prices for insurance on offices, factories, stores, cars and workers' compensation have jumped sharply in the last several years, and the cost of health insurance has been rising relentlessly even longer. Now there is a new coverage to consider: insurance against terrorist attacks.

TD 

As a result, small-business owners are combing through their policies, looking for coverages that can be cut or eliminated to help lower costs.

For years, prices were relatively stable, and reviewing coverages did not seem worth the trouble. But Dale Huffman, an Allstate agent in Orange, Calif., near Los Angeles, says that when he talks with small-business owners now, ''They're always asking, 'How can you save me money?' ''

It turns out there are several ways insurance costs can either be cut or -- more likely -- kept from rising drastically.

One frequently mentioned solution is to cut overall property coverage. But slicing off, say, 20 percent of the coverage on a $300,000 building in suburban New York would save less than $500 in premiums and leave an owner with a shortfall of $60,000 in case of a disastrous fire. Cutting coverage for liability lawsuits is not recommended either, because the added risk far outweighs the benefits. In some packages, for example, in which $2 million in liability coverage is standard, giving up $1 million in coverage saves only about $150 in premiums.

Insurance companies generally refuse to underinsure. A far better tactic, experts say, is to raise the deductible, thus making it possible to reduce premiums up to 30 percent in exchange for paying the first $10,000 in losses.

Owners of small businesses can also cut costs by removing themselves from their company's workers' compensation coverage. The coverage is not mandatory for them, as it is for employees. If they are hurt, owners can pay themselves. Or they can substitute disability coverage, which is usually more costly but provides higher payments when an injury or death occurs. And some owners have been shifting more of the cost of health insurance to their employees, as well as reducing benefits.

In a survey of small businesses, the National Federation of Independent Business, an advocacy group in Washington, found that about 34 percent of respondents increased the share their employees pay for health insurance; 30 percent increased their deductible amounts on all kinds of insurance; and 17 percent reduced or dropped some coverage on all kinds of insurance.

''The important thing is to strike the right balance,'' said Dick McKenna, an Allstate agent in Roslyn Heights, N.Y. ''You're courting disaster if you take a bigger deductible than you can comfortably handle. And for big losses you want to be fully covered.''

Small businesses are not regarded as prime targets for terrorists. In a survey by the Hartford Financial Services Group, 42 percent of respondents said they were more concerned about natural disasters like hurricanes and tornadoes; and 28 percent ranked terrorism as a main worry. But because the risk is slight, prices for terrorism coverage for small businesses are low, and many are buying it. In some cases, terrorism insurance can add as little as $50 to a small business's insurance bill, said Mike Hughes, a senior vice president in charge of coverage for small businesses at Safeco, the Seattle-based insurer.

Another way to lower premiums is through safety programs. In Los Angeles, Uni World Tensuke, a seafood company that is a unit of True World Foods of San Francisco, began paying bonuses to the drivers of its 27 trucks and cars for maintaining clean records. At the same time, it doubled its deductible to $1,000 and stopped filing claims for most damage under $2,000. Claims have fallen sharply, said MacKay Holmes, a senior manager at True World, and premiums for the fleet have dropped by roughly two-thirds, to about $40,000 a year.

Jerry Ghinelli, the owner of Total Media, a video and audiotape distributor in South Hackensack, N.J., has not been able to keep his premiums with the Hartford from rising. They have jumped 50 percent in three years to about $27,000 a year. But he figured they would be even higher if he did not pay so much attention to safety.

Mr. Ghinelli said he paid his driver a monthly bonus for avoiding accidents and tickets. In the company's building, he has nonskid mats in the hallways, deadbolts on the doors, security cameras and an alarm system that sends a signal to his personal beeper. He regularly gets his fire extinguishers and lighted exit signs checked. In 13 years he has filed one claim -- for about $5,000 for two fake checks.

''I'd rather not have to call my agent and say, 'Alan, I have a problem,' '' Mr. Ghinelli said. ''If I had a lot of claims, the premiums would go up more and they may drop you.''

A business owner can often get away with underinsuring because insurance companies do not inspect every property. But the more an owner shaves off the top of the coverage, the greater the financial danger. In most states, if the insurance coverage falls below 80 percent of the value of the property, insurers are allowed to cut back proportionately on what they pay in claims. If a building is insured for $150,000, but its real value turns out to be $300,000, the insurer is required to pay only 50 percent of any claim. After discovering this kind of deception, Mr. McKenna said, the insurer will be unlikely to renew.

These days, even insuring for the full value can be a risk. Until a few years ago, a standard property policy paid for the replacement of a shop or a factory. If a shop was insured for, say, $300,000, and the rebuilding cost was $360,000, the insurance company would simply cover the cost. But as losses rose, insurers cut back.

Now, if the owner of a photography shop in a brick building in, say, Manhassett, N.Y., bought $300,000 in coverage from Allstate for a premium of roughly $6,700, the payback in a total loss would be $300,000. If the reconstruction cost more, the owner would have to pay the difference.

But for an extra $100 per $100,000 of coverage, or $300 for the photo shop, the money available for replacement would rise 25 percent to $375,000. On these policies, the basic insured amount automatically rises annually to match inflation in construction costs. The premium also rises.

Some of the steepest price increases have been for coverage of building contractors. Many insurers have dropped the coverage, saying they lose money on it. Those who still provide it have raised their prices. Big contractors, faced with higher insurance costs, have increased their deductible amounts and begun demanding higher coverages of subcontractors working for them.

John Gregory, the president of Summit Tile and Marble, a company in Woodinville, Wash., with 10 employees, including Mr. Gregory and two other owners, said his company was forced to double its liability to $2 million. His insurance bill with Safeco jumped 40 percent to $10,000 this year, he said, even though he cut the company's auto insurance ''to the bare minimum.''

The three owners are considering dropping workers' compensation coverage for themselves. When they started 14 years ago, they went without that insurance, but Glenn Cooper, one partner, said they were hesitant to drop it.

''When you start out,'' he said, ''you have only a wife and maybe a kid. Now when you have a wife and maybe two or three kids, you've got a lot more responsibility. Hence, you need to take less risk. We're looking for some kind of alternative, something that would be less expensive.''

Part of the reason insurance is such a concern for small businesses is that ''you've got to have it,'' said William J. Dennis Jr., a senior research fellow at the National Federation of Independent Business Education Foundation.

''The alternative is to go bare,'' he said. ''If you've got any kind of borrowed money, you can't do that because of the lenders. And if you have any kind of assets, you don't want to do that. Because of the economic situation, small businesses can't raise prices. So they end up eating the increased costs. All the alternatives are bad.''

Drawing (Matt Collins)

CLM THE ECONOMY
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  A Few Bright Spots, and Lots of Hanging On and Hunkering Down
BY  By BERNARD STAMLER
WC 1098 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 3, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

HARRY DUNCAN is the president of a mortgage banking company in Sacramento. John Chua is the president of a company, also in Sacramento, that develops Web sites for the Internet. Iris Shokoff has a direct marketing media buying agency in New York. And Carolyn Brinkmeyer is a travel agent in Centennial, Colo.

All are entrepreneurs. With fewer than 500 employees each, they operate companies that are considered small businesses, insofar as that term is defined by the United States Small Business Administration.

TD 

How are they doing? For Mr. Duncan, business is better than ever. Mr. Chua has chopped his work force by a quarter, but is hanging on. Ms. Shokoff is doing all right, as other companies' troubles produce bargains for her customers. Ms. Brinkmeyer is going out of business. With the exception of Mr. Duncan, every company is doing its best to cut costs and wait for the economy to rebound.

The state of small business, in other words, depends on the business.

Given the sour economy, the health of a business nowadays is ''all about sector and location,'' said William C. Dunkelberg, the chief economist of the National Federation of Independent Business, an advocacy group in Washington.

Mr. Dunkelberg, who conducts a monthly survey of small-business owners, said that lately there was a lot of pessimism among the companies polled. But he does not ascribe that to the companies' sizes, he said; in difficult times, smaller enterprises ''suffer through the same kinds of problems as big businesses that are in the same field.''

On the other hand, most small businesses are also service companies. These days, such companies are generally having an easier time than those in manufacturing, the sector that has been hardest hit in the downturn, Mr. Dunkelberg said.

Even in the service sector, however, the health of a business is shaped by broader economic forces, and what Mr. Dunkelberg calls the ''subset of services'' that a firm provides.

In retail, store margins are in general being squeezed by fierce price competition. But some retailers are being hit harder than others, like merchants at airports, who are among those most drastically affected, said Randall S. Eisenberg, the chairman of the Turnaround Management Association, a trade group of bankruptcy professionals, and a senior managing director with FTI Consulting in New York. That is not just because of lower prices, he said, but because of another outside force: the precipitous drop in air travel.

Meanwhile, although a poor economy means that merger and acquisition lawyers may be in a slump, it also means that bankruptcy lawyers are doing quite well, said Leonard Z. Eppel, a turnaround specialist at Financial Resource Associates in Cincinnati. So are businesses that deal with residential real estate.

TAKE Vitek Mortgage Group, the company in Sacramento founded by Harry Duncan and a friend 16 years ago. Nowadays, Vitek's business is better than ever. It wrote $730 million worth of mortgages last year, according to Mr. Duncan, and grew from 75 employees to 110. This year, ''we had the best first quarter ever,'' he said, even though he had been afraid that interest rates might rise and that the company's volume would go down.

''It just hasn't happened,'' he said.

For Carolyn Brinkmeyer, the travel agent in Colorado, the opposite is true. Although she concedes that hers has always been a tough industry, it has gotten infinitely worse during the five years she has operated her business, Serenity Travel. Between the rise of direct ticket sales by airlines, online ticket services and the dropoff in travel, ''it felt like a constant battle,'' Ms. Brinkmeyer said. ''When I calculated how many hours I was working versus what I was making, I thought I needed my head examined.''

So, like thousands of other travel agents nationwide, she has given up. Earlier this year, she closed her business and is now only handling existing bookings.

Iris Shokoff's company, Iris Shokoff Associates, offers direct market media services to other businesses. Her customers include book and record clubs, a gourmet food business and Guthy-Renker, a telemarketer. Though ''things are not necessarily fabulous,'' Ms. Shokoff said, she is predicting ''a good year.''

Her company negotiates for newspaper and magazine space and radio and television time for clients' advertisements, and it has benefited from a softness in media markets, especially among print outlets. ''We can cut deals,'' she said. ''Costs are less now than they were in the dot-com era, when prices were outrageous.''

But although the Internet bust has been advantageous to Ms. Shokoff, it has not done much for John Chua, the president of Digital Gear, a Web site development company. Most of Digital Gear's customers are ''big to medium-size companies that are having challenges,'' Mr. Chua said. Because the main issue for these companies is cash flow, Mr. Chua said that he also had to become more flexible about pricing and payment.

The company, which began feeling the effects of the dot-com meltdown in 2001, has shrunk from about 20 full-time employees to 15, and it no longer uses expensive independent contractors. Instead, Mr. Chua said, he often hires temporary workers, who can be hired for less.

''We are very careful to watch expenses,'' he said.

If there is one common feature, after size, that most small businesses share today, it is a bunker mentality. If growth was the mantra in the 90's, prudence and restraint are the watchwords now.

''Small firms have to be more responsive and more nimble than ever,'' said Barbara Beck, the executive vice president of United States and Canadian operations for Manpower, the temporary-employment company. Her company provides interim staffing to numerous small businesses, Mr. Chua's among them.

Manpower also conducts a quarterly employment outlook survey, polling thousands of companies, big and small. Its most recent report indicated that in 8 of 10 categories, hiring intentions were softer for the current quarter than they were three months earlier.

Small companies, Ms. Beck said, are assessing their hiring needs more carefully than ever before, and that is unlikely to change soon.

That is a conclusion that dovetails with the results of small business surveys recently conducted by Mr. Dunkelberg. And it sounds pretty accurate to small-company owners like Mr. Chua.

''My feeling is that we will be all right in the end,'' Mr. Chua said. ''We just need to hunker down for the next couple of years.''

CLM VENTURE CAPITAL
SE  Small Business; Section G
HD  Money for Start-Ups: Tight, Getting Tighter
BY  By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ
WC 1131 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 5, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

PALO ALTO, Calif. -- IN March 2000, it took only 10 days for Thomas E. Rowley, a Silicon Valley entrepreneur for more than two decades, to raise $27 million for Counterpane Internet Security, his eighth start-up company. Mr. Rowley's next round of financing was completed in January 2003 -- after eight months and 102 pitches that raised $20 million.

And most of his fellow entrepreneurs would consider him lucky.

TD 

''This is an awful time for companies looking to recapitalize,'' Mr. Rowley said.

It is not that the boom times, when venture capitalists often avoided performing due diligence on companies in their rush to close deals, are gone. And it is not that entrepreneurs must once again show that they have proven business models, strong customer prospects and experienced management teams. That, after all, was how venture capital always worked, until the bubble. The big problem for new companies now is that even when they do everything right, there is precious little money to win.

The amount of money that venture capitalists have sunk into privately held technology companies has declined every quarter since the start of 2000, according to the MoneyTree survey, a study of venture capital investment activity compiled by PricewaterhouseCoopers, Venture Economics and the National Venture Capital Association. The survey's figures for the most recent quarter, ending March 31, had the smallest number of venture capital deals, 623, since the summer quarter of 1996.

Moreover, there has been one public stock offering for a venture capital-backed company since January, down from 262 for 2000, the MoneyTree survey reported.

Last August, Jonathan Abrams, a 33-year-old software engineer, began a start-up business, Friendster, an online dating and networking service. He said that he was ''bootstrapping'' the business, running it from his apartment in Sunnyvale, Calif. ''I'm not even bothering to schmooze with venture capitalists right now,'' Mr. Abrams said.

Mr. Abrams is more fortunate than many technology entrepreneurs. He said that he was able to raise some money -- an amount he only said was less than $500,000 -- from friends and former colleagues.

Raising capital from ''angels,'' wealthy individuals who invest in start-up companies, has long been an avenue for brand-new technology businesses, but the competition for this financing has become tougher. Ian Sobieski, who coordinates the activities of one such Silicon Valley network, the Band of Angels, said that the group had always heard pitches from entrepreneurs who were turning an idea into a company. Lately, however, he said they were seeing more venture-backed technology businesses that have been around for a few years and need another round of financing, but cannot raise the full sum from venture capitalists.

Many young companies, unable to raise money from angels or venture capitalists, are financing their dreams themselves. Their owners are taking the maximum on their credit cards, cashing out I.R.A.'s and acquiring second mortgages. A multimedia software designer at one Bay Area business, who asked not to be identified, was helping to support his two partners and paying for the lawyer to patent their work by spending all his time composing music for electronic games.

In a big shift, many entrepreneurs here are turning to the government as a financing option. Workshops on obtaining government grants and contracts have become as common in Silicon Valley in 2003 as they were rare during the boom.

For those who find venture capital financing, the investments are smaller, and the venture capitalists expect that money to last years, not months.

Matt Glickman, the chief executive and a co-founder of Merced Systems in Redwood City, Calif., raised a first round of $3.5 million in venture capital financing at the end of 2001. As part of the pitch for the company, which sells software to monitor call centers, Mr. Glickman said he presented testimonials from two customers, including a division of the General Electric Company.

Since raising the money, Merced Systems has gone 17 months without seeking another round, quickly increasing its sales while pinching every penny. That was different a few years ago, when Mr. Glickman was running an online retailer, BabyCenter .com, which is now a subsidiary of Johnson & Johnson. During the late 90's, BabyCenter raised new rounds of venture capital every nine months. ''Merced Systems has to be focused on reaching sustainability,'' he said.

There are still a few instances of the go-for-broke mentality. WildBlue, a communications company in Greenwood Village, Colo., which is trying to develop a system to deliver wireless broadband access to the Internet through new satellite technology, closed a $156 million round of financing last month. The deal included new investors, Intelsat and the National Rural Telecommunications Cooperative, in addition to previous investors -- Liberty Media Corporation and Kleiner, Perkins, Caufield & Byers, the Silicon Valley venture capital firm.

Although WildBlue raised more than $300 million before 2001, it had run out of cash one year later and suspended the development of its network. ''We then spent all of our time trying to raise money,'' said Thomas Moore, WildBlue's chief executive.

The company also had to wait for rivals to die. ''It took two years to let that play through,'' said Russell Siegelman, a partner at Kleiner, Perkins who sits on WildBlue's board of directors.

David Cowan, a partner at Bessemer Venture Partners in Menlo Park, Calif., said that in addition to new wireless technologies, he saw only a few other niches still attracting venture capital. One is computer network security, he said, which is why he invested in Mr. Rowley's company, Counterpane. Counterpane sells systems to monitor computer networks of businesses.

But Mr. Cowan, who has invested in Counterpane's previous rounds of financing and is on its board, also took into account how Mr. Rowley and others on the company's executive team managed start-ups through hard economic times. ''The prevailing metric now is capital efficiency,'' Mr. Cowan said.

Howard Clowes, a lawyer at Gray Cary who handles venture capital deals, said he told first-time entrepreneurs not to bother venture capitalists until they hooked up with an experienced manager.

Mr. Rowley said that such demands are a sign that the venture capital business was returning to normal. As a result, Counterpane was given a lower market value in its last round, he said, declining to specify the amount. Although that was painful to accept, he added that it was better than the alternative: no money at all.

SE  National Desk; Section A
HD  Bush, Returning From Ranch, Resumes Effort for Tax Cut
BY  By DAVID E. SANGER
WC 678 words
PD 6 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 25, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

LITTLE ROCK, Ark., May 5 -- Lacking the votes he needs in the Senate for his tax cut plan, President Bush stopped here today on his way back to Washington from his ranch in Texas to campaign for the plan and to put direct pressure on a Democratic opponent, Senator Blanche Lincoln of Arkansas.

Mr. Bush never mentioned Ms. Lincoln's name, but he urged his audience -- mostly Republican supporters and small-business owners in a state that he narrowly won in 2000 -- to contact their Congressional delegations to push his plan.

TD 

Ms. Lincoln, a member of the Senate Finance Committee, which will take up the legislation this week. issued a statement this afternoon that left the president little room for hope.

''Arkansans need to know,'' she said, ''that the president's proposal to eliminate taxes on dividends is simply too expensive and just won't work for them.''

Mr. Bush, in his speech here and in a similar one on Friday in Santa Clara, Calif., described his plans as job generators, especially for small businesses. Today, he appeared to go slightly further, at one moment describing his proposal as one of a series of tax reductions to come.

''It's not just a onetime shot,'' he said.

''We're talking $2,000 a year until we lower taxes again,'' he added, a reference to the savings projected for one of the owners of a small business at the speech.

Mr. Bush said he was ''concerned about the deficit,'' an issue that has given pause to many in his own party, but he added he was ''first and foremost concerned about that person looking for a job.''

Tonight, Senator Charles E. Grassley of Iowa, chairman of the Finance Committee, told fellow Republicans that he would offer a skeletal tax plan of his own on Tuesday that would include neither a reduction in taxes on stock dividends, a central element of the Bush proposal, nor new aid to the states, an important issue to some swing-state senators.

Other senators said Mr. Grassley hoped to buy two days of time to pressure other senators before his panel meets to draft legislation on Thursday. But so far, senators seem locked into positions, and that means the president is at least two votes shy of a majority in the Senate.

On Tuesday, the House Ways and Means Committee plans to take up and almost certainly approve a $550 billion 10-year tax cut that follows the president's prescription with one important exception. Instead of eliminating the tax on dividends as the president proposed, the House bill would reduce the tax on dividends and capital gains, to 15 percent. Dividends are now taxed at the same rate as income from salaries and wages, as high as 38.6 percent. Most capital gains are taxed at 18 percent or 20 percent.

In the Senate, Mr. Grassley is bound by a budget that limits the total 10-year tax cut to $350 billion. He was under pressure today to include in that package some measure on stock dividends, said people at a meeting of all Republican senators. Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi said there was overwhelming sentiment among fellow Republicans for the proposal.

Senator Max Baucus of Montana, the senior Democrat on the Finance Committee, who is usually in close touch with Mr. Grassley, said he expected Mr. Grassley to add such a measure, perhaps a temporary cut, a process called sunsetting.

As of tonight, there did not seem to be enough votes on the committee for its approval. The panel has 11 Republicans and 10 Democrats. All the Democrats could be expected to vote against it, and Senator Olympia J. Snowe, Republican of Maine, said she, too, would vote against a sunset provision.

''It's just a gimmick,'' Ms. Snowe said.

CLM COMMERCIAL REAL ESTATE
SE  Business/Financial Desk; Section C
HD  Bucking the Tide, eBay Expands in Silicon Valley
BY  By TERRY PRISTIN
WC 1423 words
PD 14 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 10, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

SAN JOSE, Calif., May 8 -- Last month, eBay, the Internet auction company, brought some welcome news to a city and region that have been suffering from one of the worst office market slumps in the nation. The eight-year-old company, which had toyed with giving up its headquarters here, announced that it would not only keep its West San Jose complex but would also buy a 32-acre campus from the software developer Novell on the opposite side of town.

The eBay deal, which still must undergo an environmental review, would allow the company to expand gradually, thereby avoiding the mistakes made by other highflying Silicon Valley companies that overcommitted themselves during the economic boom of the late 1990's and are now stuck with empty or only partly occupied buildings because of the slide in the technology industry.

TD 

Almost half of the 511,000 square feet of space in the five aluminum-and-steel buildings of the Novell campus is leased, leaving just 260,000 square feet for eBay to occupy in the immediate future. The company won several unusually generous concessions from the city that will allow further expansion to as much as two million square feet.

But should eBay stop growing, said the city's economic development director, Paul Krutko, the company will be able to sell its development rights. ''This deal also provides an exit strategy for eBay,'' Mr. Krutko said.

Though the eBay deal lifted spirits, especially among city officials, it will have little effect on vacancy rates in the Silicon Valley, which by its broadest definition includes Santa Clara County, where San Jose is located; San Mateo County; and Fremont, in southern Alameda County. Santa Clara County alone has lost 200,000 jobs since 2000, about half of the job loss in the entire state. Evidence of the downturn is everywhere -- from the empty parking lots in front of office parks that are only a few years old, to the quiet atmosphere of downtown San Jose and the easily flowing traffic along freeways that were once congested.

A newly completed 17-story blue-and-tan skyscraper near the San Jose Convention Center that can be seen for miles has no tenants for its 380,000 square feet, and Boston Properties has shelved plans to put up three buildings across the street with a total 850,000 square feet, said Bob Pester, the regional manager for the developer.

Of the valley's 166.7 million square feet of space for high-technology research and development, 21.3 percent was vacant as of the end of the last quarter, compared with 2.3 percent for the peak of the market in the second quarter of 2000, said Charles W. Harry Jr., the managing director of the analytics advisory group for Cushman & Wakefield, the national real estate company. The vacancy rate for the office market of about 70.9 million square feet stands at 27.8 percent, compared with 1.8 percent three years ago, he said. The vacancy rate for the high-technology market is the worst it has been since the second quarter of 1993 while the office market has not been this soft since the first quarter of 1987, Mr. Harry said.

The numbers may not be the worst ever, but with 14 million square feet of high-technology space added in just a few years, many people in the industry expect the recovery to take a frustratingly long time. ''This is the worst market in the Silicon Valley that we've ever seen,'' said Raymond W. Marino, the president of Mission West Properties, a publicly traded real estate investment trust that manages 7.8 million square feet, primarily in Silicon Valley. ''In all prior downturns, we've always had a new company that was just coming into its own. This time, everyone is just stalled.''

Average annual asking rents in the research and development market were $15.41 a square foot in the last quarter, compared with a peak of $57.13 at the end of 2000, Mr. Harry said. In the office market, rents have dropped to $27.34 from a height of $69.68, according to Cushman & Wakefield. C. Michael Kamm, the chief executive of BT Commercial, a Northern California company, said ''rents have fallen to levels we haven't seen in seven or eight years.''

As their need for space evaporated, some companies paid dearly to get out of their leases. It cost Handspring, the personal organizer manufacturer, $75 million to extricate itself from plans to move from Mountain View to Sunnyvale, said Brian Jaquot, a company spokesman.

But in Silicon Valley, as in other high-technology areas, the typical lease runs only 5 to 7 years rather than 10 to 15 years. So while space available for sublease accounts for a substantial portion of the vacancies right now -- 13.2 million of the 35 million square feet of empty research-and-development space, according to BT Commercial -- many of those leases will expire in a year or two, shifting the burden for filling the space from tenants to landlords.

In anticipation, many landlords and tenants are working deals to ''blend and extend'' their leases, in brokers' jargon. Mr. Marino said he was working out one such deal with a San Jose technology company that needed only 45,000 of the 120,000 square feet that it had leased until September 2004. Under the proposed new terms, the company would extend its lease for the 45,000 square feet at market rates through May 31, 2008, and the $2.3 million in rent due on the remaining 75,000 square feet would be spread out (with an undisclosed amount of interest added) over the five-year term.

One measure of how bad things are, said James J. Curtis III, a partner in Bristol Group, a real estate investment company, is that some buildings used to command annual leases of $70 a square foot (without utilities); now space is being sold at the same price.

That is slightly more than the South Bay Development Company of Campbell, Calif., paid for an empty 359,000-square-foot building in the city of Santa Clara that it acquired in August from Nortel Networks. Built in 1975, the building was completely remodeled in 1999 at a cost of $100 a square foot, said Mark J. Regoli, a South Bay partner.

In November, his company bought a 78,000-square-foot property in Sunnyvale for $5 million; the seller, Qwest communications, had paid $25.2 million for the building the previous December, he said. ''For us,'' Mr. Regoli said, ''we view the next 6 to 24 months as a good opportunity to accumulate.''

Real estate prices have fallen so far that the 3Com Corporation, a leading maker of computer-networking equipment, recently took its 900,000-square-foot Santa Clara campus off the market. Brokers and investors said the company could not even get an offer of $100 a square foot, about one-third of what the complex cost to build. EBay has not disclosed what it paid for the landscaped Novell campus, which includes a reflecting pool; tennis, volleyball and basketball courts; jogging paths; a gymnasium; and an espresso cafe as well as a cafeteria. But one real estate specialist familiar with the negotiations said it fetched just under $185 a square foot.

San Jose city officials were so eager to keep eBay in town that they abandoned their plans to buy an adjacent 15-acre vacant site from Novell to use in connection with a major airport expansion program. Instead, Novell will sell the land to eBay.

As an Internet company, eBay does not generate sales tax revenue for San Jose, but it has 1,500 employees who spend their paychecks locally, Mr. Krutko said. ''EBay is what you call a 'gazelle company,' '' Mr. Krutko said, ''It doubles in size every five years. There are very few of those companies around.''

CLM AFTEREFFECTS: RECONSTRUCTION
SE  Foreign Desk; Section A
HD  U.S. Officials Ground an Entrepreneur in Iraq
BY  By EDMUND L. ANDREWS
WC 1203 words
PD 15 May 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
PG Page 22, Column 1
LA English
CY  c. 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

BAGHDAD, Iraq, May 14 -- As the Bush administration rushes to jump-start the reconstruction of postwar Iraq, it may want to consider the recent woes of Pete Henderson.

Mr. Henderson, a South African entrepreneur, thought last week that he had lined up everything he needed to start Air Baghdad, the first commercial air service into and out of Iraq since the war.

TD 

Mr. Henderson had flight approval from American military commanders to operate his first route between Baghdad and Amman, Jordan. He had a plane and crew. Because armed gangs are attacking and killing road travelers with increasing frequency, he had throngs of customers ready to pay one-way fares of $650. He even had insurance from Lloyd's of London.

But just as the first passengers were ready to board his 50-seat commuter plane last Thursday, Air Baghdad was grounded. The decision boiled down to this: he was moving too early to make a profit and might get a jump on his competitors.

''No flights for profit or commercial purposes will be approved,'' wrote Lt. Col. David Jones, the senior adviser on Iraqi civil aviation at the Office of Reconstruction and Humanitarian Assistance, in an e-mail message to Mr. Henderson on Tuesday. Any attempt to cloak flights as ''humanitarian,'' he added, ''will be dealt with harshly.''

Mr. Henderson, sitting in a tent that he has equipped with electricity and high-speed Internet access by satellite, said he was dumbfounded.

''I'm putting up my own money,'' he said. ''I'm offering a service that is badly needed and could save lives. I would have thought people would say, 'Great, glad you're here.' ''

Many other entrepreneurs are having the same experience. With ordinary telephone service all but nonexistent in much of the country, and likely to remain so for months, many companies want to roll out cellular phone systems that they say could be operating within weeks.

But American administrators say they will not license any commercial mobile telephone services, even temporarily, until they have a comprehensive regulatory system to govern issues from what they call ''interoperability'' to telephone numbers.

Meanwhile, Iraq's biggest pharmaceutical company, Dofar Pharmaceuticals, says it has large inventories of antibiotics and other medicines but remains unable to sell them because it cannot figure out whom to deal with in the American-led office of reconstruction.

That office, which is supposed to oversee the rebuilding of Iraq, is in the midst of an overhaul. Jay Garner, the retired lieutenant general who has been in charge, is being replaced by L. Paul Bremer III, a presidential envoy.

''It's been very difficult,'' said one exasperated American business consultant. ''You can waste three hours a day walking around the halls trying to talk to people.''

The upshot is that Iraqis are left to rely on their own creativity and limited resources. Small shopkeepers, though enduring horrendous economic conditions, are paying $700 or more for satellite phones so they can order products from other countries or from across town. These phones are not only expensive but also tricky to use because they have to be pointed at a satellite to receive calls.

To make up for the absence of Iraqi television, dark since the war, people have been buying satellite dishes and receivers to pull in signals from Iran, Qatar and Abu Dhabi.

But home-grown adaptability goes only so far. Many essential services, from telephones to airlines, require government approval. Many others, including the rebuilding of buildings, utilities and telephone networks, require government money.

Mr. Henderson, 41, was not looking for government money, but he knew he needed approval. A former television cameraman, he spent most of his career supplying crews and logistical services to news organizations covering the first Persian Gulf war, the Balkan wars and various conflicts in Africa.

But after selling his company, Newsforce, to France Telecom several years ago, Mr. Henderson settled into comfortable homes in Kenya and Britain. After the United States ousted the Iraqi government, he arrived here to set up satellite communications to the headquarters of the Iraqi National Congress, a party built by returning Iraqi exiles under Ahmad Chalabi.

The urge to set up a commuter air service to Jordan occurred to Mr. Henderson almost five weeks ago.

The highway from Amman to Baghdad has long been a favored route to Iraq, but it has become increasingly dangerous. In the past few weeks, trucks owned by CNN, CBS and The Associated Press have been attacked, and one CNN employee was shot in the hand.

Business executives and journalists have become increasingly reluctant to make the drive.

Mr. Henderson first pitched his idea for Air Baghdad to the American Regional Air Mobilization Control Center in Qatar, which approved his plans for a round-trip flight for last Thursday.

But he was blocked by Colonel Jones, who told him that the reconstruction office was waiting for an ''assessment'' of the airport, due June 1, and that final clearance might take another month after that.

As discussions continued, said Mr. Henderson, Colonel Jones argued that Air Baghdad would get an unfair advantage over competitors if it began operations early. Mr. Henderson then proposed limiting the flights to journalists and designating the flights a humanitarian effort.

But that fell on deaf ears as well, and on Tuesday Mr. Henderson was pessimistic about carrying off his next scheduled flight on Thursday.

Other airlines are also trying to offer service to and from Baghdad. British Air said it would be ready to resume flights to the capital by June 1, though it appears unlikely they will stay on schedule if American officials insist on completing their airport assessment by June 1.

In addition, at least two wealthy Iraqi families are organizing their own air service.

The roadblocks to offering mobile telephone service may be even more daunting. Despite the near obliteration of telephone service in Baghdad, and only limited service in other cities, American officials have so far approved only the construction of a private network that could be used by United States government workers, Iraqi government ministries and perhaps some aid agencies.

Stephen Browning, the senior adviser to the Ministry of Transportation and Communication, said this week that commercial services would not be approved for ''probably two months, at the minimum.''

Mr. Browning said American officials were reluctant to give valuable radio licenses before an interim Iraqi government was in place.

AT&T, Turkcell of Turkey and several Iraqi entrepreneurs are among many groups that have already organized truckloads of cellular equipment in nearby countries that they could deploy around Baghdad and turn into a functioning system.

Asked why American officials could not simply award cellular licenses that would only be valid for a year or two, allowing the new Iraqi government to issue permanent licenses, Mr. Browning said the decision was outside his control.

''These are decisions that are going to be made in Washington,'' he said.

CLM HOME FRONT
SE Job Market; SECT10
HD Measuring the Time Needed to Succeed
BY By Anthony DePalma
WC 863 words
PD 29 June 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

THE secret to Jagdish (Jay) Patel's formidable stamina and reliability is a strong constitution, the ability to get by on six hours sleep and the jigger of scotch that he says he drinks every night before retiring. Clears the head, he says, and keeps a small businessman from ''thinking too much'' about his business.

As owner of Henry's Fine Foods, an old-fashioned small grocery store in Verona, N.J., Mr. Patel, 43, keeps a schedule that would seem punishing to anyone but another small businessman. His store is open every day of the week. And holidays too, including Christmas. He is even open during snowstorms, like the time last winter when Verona got hit with 29 inches. He slept in a cot in the backroom and opened the next morning, as usual, just after five.

TD 

Since Mr. Patel bought Henry's in 2000, not a single day has gone by without the front door opening and the cash register clanging. But that achievement pales besides that of his predecessors in the store. Henry Percevault opened the store on Bloomfield Avenue in 1930 and did business each and every day for years. His son Joseph took over and kept the streak going until his own retirement in 2000, when he sold the business to Mr. Patel.

In all, Henry's has opened every day since the beginning of 1930, a total of 73 years, or 26,843 days and counting. It is probably some kind of record, and if it's not, it should be.

Mr. Patel, who started working on his father's businesses in India when he was 16, expects to keep up his brutal schedule for 10 more years, by which time his three children will be on their own. Then, he said, he and his wife will ''just take a rest,'' though he expects he will continue to get up at 4 a.m. out of habit.

Someone once defined an entrepreneur as a person willing to work 16 hours a day so they don't have to work 8 hours a day for someone else. Managing time is one of the single most important determinants, besides money, of a new business's success.

''Time and time management are definitely on the list of success factors,'' for a small business, said Kris Solie-Johnson, president of the the American Institute of Small Business, a book company in Minneapolis that publishes ''How to Set Up Your Own Small Business,'' an 850-page reference work carried by many local libraries.

As the national economy has slowed, the number of people starting their own business has risen. According to the United States Small Business Administration, 550,100 new firms were opened in 2002.

But it takes more than a clever idea or original concept to build a new business. Only about half of newly created firms survive at least four years, and miscalculating the amount of time required is to blame for many failures.

''We always advise people that before going into business they should put together a business plan that gives a more realistic sense of what the real time commitment is going to be,'' said Miguel Centeno, a public affairs officer for the Small Business Administration office in New York.

The Small Business Administration also runs a counseling service for entrepreneurs called the Service Corps of Retired Executives, or SCORE, ( www.score.org[http://www.score.org]) which can help answer questions and give advice.

''Running a small business isn't hard,'' said Martin R. Lehman, a retired retailer with SCORE, ''it's 25 hours a day.''

For those who do not have the stamina for 80-hour weeks with or without scotch, Ms. Solie-Johnson offered a few time management hints. She uses an online payroll service that produces time savings enough to more than compensate for the small fee she pays. And she turns off her cellphone while driving the 45 minutes to and from work.

Henry J. Coleman, owner of Alchemy Answers, an online service that provides information and advice for small businessmen ( www.alchemyanswers.com[http://www.alchemyanswers.com]) along with several retail businesses, said he learned some fundamental rules about time management after putting in 100-hour weeks trying to get a stock brokerage going.

''The lesson I learned is that time management is not deciding what to do but what not to do,'' he said. ''Once you do that, it's amazing how much time you create.''

Instead of putting together a to-do list, Mr. Coleman, who now runs five different companies, prepares a list of things that are not his responsibility. ''I knew from Day 1 that my time would be finite,'' he said. ''I refused to build an enterprise where the only way to grow was for me to put in one more hour. Once you start down that road, there is no end.''

Photo: Jagdish (Jay) Patel keeps Henry's Fine Foods in Verona, N.J., open every day of the year. His predecessors did the same. Henry's has been open every day since 1930. (Dith Pran/The New York Times)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTW
HD Fear of Recession Fading In Japan Business Survey
BY By KEN BELSON
WC 699 words
PD 2 July 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

TOKYO, July 1 -- Pessimism about the Japanese economy is slowly fading, a central bank survey of business executives showed today, as manufacturers prepare for an increase in orders from overseas for their cars, electronics and machinery.

The Bank of Japan's quarterly ''tankan'' report suggests that concerns about the world's second-largest economy sliding into recession are overblown. Though the large manufacturers that typically drive economic recoveries here remain wary, they are quietly raising their profit and capital spending goals.

TD 

The poll's index of major manufacturers rose five points to minus 5, which means pessimists still outnumbered optimists but by a smaller margin. The companies' financial outlooks were stronger than the predictions of private economists, who expected confidence to slip.

The improvement, however mild, comes as disruptions from the SARS virus and the war in Iraq fade, while oil prices slide and stock prices extend a two-month rally. The United States economy is also improving, good news for exporters like Honda Motor, the Matsushita Electric Industrial Company and other companies that earn most of their profits overseas.

While corporate managers expect no improvement in confidence by the time of the next survey in September, manufacturers raised their pretax profit forecasts for the fiscal year that began in April. Some of that progress comes as companies reduce their payrolls, close factories and streamline balance sheets.

But manufacturers are also preparing for new sales by planning to invest more in factories and operations. Large manufacturers, for instance, expect their capital spending to increase 11.5 percent this year.

''The economy is still at the mercy of exports,'' said Ryo Hino, an economist at J. P. Morgan Securities in Tokyo. ''But the numbers suggest that the momentum is stronger than we thought.''

Japan's chief financial regulator, Heizo Takenaka, said the survey's results were within expectations, while the finance minister, Masajuro Shiokawa, said the improvement at large manufacturers was ''an honest reflection'' of their restructuring efforts.

Investors viewed today's news as a bullish sign for the economy. The Nikkei 225 stock index rose 2.2 percent to 9,278, a nine-month high. The dollar slipped against the yen after the report was released, but later recouped its losses.

The tankan's optimism was seconded by a separate report that showed new car sales rising 2.6 percent in June, the ninth gain in 10 months.

Though Japan's $4.2 trillion economy is showing signs of life, the recovery is far from robust. Domestic sales remain sluggish, and residents face a difficult job market, falling incomes and higher taxes.

The main culprit is deflation, or falling prices. While price declines help consumers in search of cheaper goods, they hurt the companies that make those products.

To compensate, companies must reduce hiring and cut other fixed costs.

The worst-hit companies are those that compete with low-cost producers from China, South Korea and other Asian countries. While demand for cellphones, personal digital assistants and other electronics are strong, the prices of the components packed into these machines remain weak.

''I don't think prices will improve this year because of the macroeconomy,'' said Yoshihide Fujii, executive vice president in charge of semiconductors at Toshiba, Japan's largest chipmaker. ''We have only one choice: To cut costs to cope with this.''

Though the government has put together several antideflation spending packages and the Bank of Japan has followed an ultra-loose monetary policy for several years, prices are locked in their fifth year of decline.

A survey of business sentiment in Japan showed greater confidence in the latest quarter, a hopeful sign for its sluggish economy.

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD Confidence Survey Shows Increasing Optimism
CR By Bloomberg News
WC 292 words
PD 19 July 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 4
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

Consumer confidence rose this month as a measure of current economic conditions increased to its highest level in more than a year, a University of Michigan survey showed yesterday.

The university's preliminary July sentiment index rose to 90.3 from 89.7 last month. That was the third rise in four months. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks has risen 22 percent since a low in March, which may have contributed to higher confidence this month, economists said.

TD 

Lower interest rates and prospects of tax cuts may also be increasing optimism, paving the way for stronger consumer spending and growth that is needed before companies feel confident enough to resume hiring.

''What we are going to have is good growth, but it's not going to be a boom,'' said John Silvia, chief economist at the Wachovia Corporation in Charlotte, N.C. ''Consumers still have a lot of concerns out there. I don't see how consumers are going to write off the war or write off the job problem.''

The current conditions index, which reflects the perceptions of Americans of their financial situation and whether it is a good time to make major purchases, rose to 102.1, the highest point since May 2002. It was 94.7 in June.

The second half of the year ''will be significantly better than the first half,'' Thomas Dattilo, chief executive of the Cooper Tire & Rubber Company, the nation's second-biggest tire maker, said in an interview yesterday. ''There is going to be more money in consumers' pockets; the tax cuts will make sure of that. We certainly see an improved sentiment out there in retail.''
