SE Editorial Desk; SECTA
HD Small Businesses, Big Growth
BY By Alexander von Hoffman
WC 910 words
PD 4 September 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 23
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

CAMBRIDGE, Mass. -- Believe it or not, the little guys in the so-called outer boroughs could help revive New York City's economy. Small businesses have emerged as a strong growth sector in the city -- and Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg should find new ways to support them.

While financial services and other sectors still languish, according to the National Association of Purchasing Management-New York, small businesses, often owned and patronized by immigrants and minorities, have been growing. Leading this expansion are specialty and ethnic-food manufacturers, like the bakeries that produce rolls for high-end restaurants and sausage makers that serve immigrant neighborhoods. Over the last year the number of people these businesses employ grew by almost 8 percent -- probably more, since many firms go unreported.

TD 

Food producers are only the most visible among many small-scale industries that are thriving and poised to expand. Evidence, albeit fragmentary, suggests there is growth in construction, specialty apparel, medical instruments and luxury consumer goods like jewelry.

Unfortunately, New York offers few programs aimed at such businesses. To its credit, the city government recently announced it would develop 10 sites in Brooklyn and the Bronx for much-needed industrial space. There are also some tax incentives, like credits for utility bills and waivers for manufacturers trying to develop facilities. Reflecting New York's labyrinthine regulatory environment, the city also offers help dealing with its own agencies, including the departments of sanitation, health and police.

Most small manufacturers, however, will not use these services. They are too busy running their business, and some may fear that if they contact the government, officials will interfere with their business or workers.

So what can New York do to reach these business owners? A useful lesson can be found in Los Angeles, where in the early 1990's small manufacturers helped bring some prosperity to urban neighborhoods there.

After the devastation left by the riots of 1992, Los Angeles and California officials looked to large corporations to revive the city. As head of an ad hoc reconstruction agency, Peter Ueberroth (the former baseball commissioner and current candidate for governor of California) tried to persuade many chief executives to move some operations to the inner city. The oil, aerospace and movie industries were all staggering in the 1990's, however, and Mr. Ueberroth had little success.

But his successor, Linda Griego, sensed life in the inner city. Ms. Griego, a former chili stand operator and deputy mayor for economic development, commissioned a door-to-door survey of businesses operating in South Central Los Angeles and other forsaken areas.

To nearly everyone's surprise, inner-city Los Angeles turned out to be an industrial beehive. Its 15,000 companies employed more than 360,000 people -- mainly immigrants and minorities -- and generated more than $54 billion in sales annually of everything from tortillas to bicycling outfits. But -- as in New York -- most of the proprietors knew little about government programs and sometimes operated outside government rules.

Ms. Griego, along with the nonprofit Community Development Technologies Center and eventually the city government, adopted an unusual strategy to deal with the mom-and-pop entrepreneurs. They worked directly with the small manufacturers to help them organize trade associations, navigate government regulations and upgrade the skills of their workers. In the end, small manufactures succeeded where large industry failed: they helped rescue the economy of inner-city Los Angeles.

Of course, Los Angeles is not New York. New York is far larger -- both in terms of population and the size of its government -- and it has not relied on industry to drive its economy the way Los Angeles has. But the Bloomberg administration can certainly do more to encourage small-scale manufacturing.

To begin with, economic development officials must learn about the city's disparate manufacturers and identify the issues they face. Government officials, perhaps in cooperation with a business association or nonprofit group, should undertake a systematic study of small industry in the city -- mindful of the need for street-by-street canvassing because corporate records and permits may not reveal every business.

Having identified the most important industries, the city government could help organize trade associations. Since many small-business owners are so intently focused on their company, they are notoriously isolated from one another. Together, manufacturers can overcome their shared problems. Owners of small ethnic-food businesses in Los Angeles, for example, lobbied the state to block a steep hike in inspection fees -- something they could never have done without banding together.

New York's government should also go beyond the tax waivers and credits it now provides and actually reduce the taxes on small businesses. This would ensure that costs of operating in New York do not prevent companies from expanding -- and it would also increase the rate of tax collection because fewer businesses would dodge taxes. Finally, the Bloomberg administration should reduce the bureaucracy and red tape that often make running a legitimate enterprise in New York City a logistical nightmare.

By taking such steps, Mayor Bloomberg can unleash the energies of small-fry entrepreneurs busily operating in the boroughs -- and help them revive the economy of New York City.

Drawing (Drawing by Red Nose Studio)


RF 
Alexander von Hoffman, senior fellow at the Joint Center for Housing Studies at Harvard, is author of ''House by House, Block by Block: The Rebirth of America's Urban Neighborhoods.''

HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Confidence Rises
BY By Victor Homola (NYT)
WC 89 words
PD 26 September 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

German business confidence jumped to its highest level in two and a half years this month, according to a survey of 7,000 western German companies released by the Ifo Institute in Munich. The overall index rose to 91.9, from 90.8 in August, the highest reading since April 2001. The gain was led by increased optimism among manufacturers and retailers. Construction companies, however, became more pessimistic during the month.

Victor Homola (NYT)

CLM ECONOMIC VIEW
SE Money and Business/Financial Desk; SECT3
HD Sunny Skies For Europe? Grab That Umbrella
BY By MARK LANDLER
WC 804 words
PD 5 October 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 4
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

FRANKFURT -- TO judge by the economic surveys that are published almost daily in Europe, the Continent is solidly on the road to recovery. So why are the people who study these barometers so dour?

The trend is clear: Germany's influential Ifo index of business sentiment rose in September for the fifth consecutive month, to its highest level in more than two years.

TD 

The ZEW survey of investor confidence, released by the ZEW Center for European Economic Research, and its consumer counterpart, the GfK survey, by the market research company GfK in Nuremberg, are also showing newfound vigor.

The upward-pointing arrows are not limited to Germany. In France, the closely watched Insee survey, by the national statistics office, recorded its second consecutive monthly improvement last week, after a long stretch of gloomy results.

Yet rather than proclaim a recovery under way, most economists warn that it is too soon to declare a turn in Europe's fortunes. Part of the caution stems from experience. Most of these surveys registered similar gains in sentiment last year, stoking hopes of a recovery that never materialized. Germany fell back into a mild recession last autumn, while the rest of Europe stagnated. ''People had their fingers burned,'' said Thomas Mayer, the chief European economist at Deutsche Bank.

Hope may again be sprinting ahead of reality. ''It's too early to say a recovery has arrived,'' Mr. Mayer said. ''For that, we'd like to see a turnaround in current conditions. Unfortunately, there is a considerable disparity between expectations and conditions.''

Consider the Ifo index, which is published by the Ifo Institute, an economic research group in Munich. The index, a survey of 7,000 German companies, showed an improvement in sentiment in September. But it was driven by a rise in expectations for a better future, not on conditions today.

With the exception of a positive report on manufacturing orders last month, there is no tangible evidence -- in statistics like unemployment or retail sales -- that the German economy is moving. The disparity between emotions and evidence exists in France and other countries.

On the same day the new Insee survey was published, French officials lowered their estimates of gross domestic product in late 2002 and early 2003, which means their already modest goal of 0.5 percent growth this year is probably unattainable.

Some experts chalk this up to the normal cycle of an economic recovery. There was a similar lag between improved sentiment and tangible economic results in the United States, they said. ''Europe is at a much earlier stage,'' said David Abramson, the chief European strategist at the BCA Research Group in Montreal. Beyond issues of timing, however, the caution reflects a fear that this recovery is unusually fragile. The euro has renewed its climb against the dollar, which will put pressure on the German and French exports that are considered a crucial driver of any sustained growth in Europe.

''The euro is one of the major threats to the recovery,'' said Michael Heise, the chief economist at Allianz in Frankfurt. A sharply higher euro, he noted, could cause Europe's economies to succumb yet again.

Mr. Heise said he was encouraged that the German government had pushed ahead with politically painful reforms of the labor market, as well as of welfare and pension benefits. The government also cheered the public by accelerating a tax cut that had been scheduled for 2005.

The trouble is, too much good news on the economic front could pose a threat to the reform agenda of Chancellor Gerhard Schroder. European leaders have tended to put their reform plans in mothballs during times of robust growth because they no longer appear as necessary.

PERHAPS the easiest way to deal with the curse of rising expectations would be to pay less attention to the alphabet soup of surveys. Ifo, ZEW, and GfK came to prominence partly because of the growth of financial markets. Investors like trading on the numbers.

But they also reflect the tardiness and unreliability of the economic data released by European governments. If Paris, Berlin and Rome issued timely, dependable statistics, Mr. Mayer said, the influence of these private surveys would ebb.

As it is, he said, the surveys can even distort an economic cycle. Last year, after the flood of upbeat reports, the European Central Bank sent out hawkish signals about interest rates. That spooked investors, whose reactions may have contributed to the subsequent relapse of the economy.

''We need a more balanced diet,'' Mr. Mayer said. ''If we live off these surveys, we'll get indigestion.''

CLM SMALL BUSINESS
SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD 'Agnostic' Ad Agency Finds Niche
BY By FARA WARNER
WC 1479 words
PD 30 October 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 7
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

SAN FRANCISCO -- Eleven Inc., an upstart advertising agency that not only survived the Internet meltdown but went on to thrive in an industry that has been battered for years by weak corporate spending, has a big display chart that almost always makes prospective clients sit up.

It shows how much more expensive it would be for them to pay for a few seconds of television advertising than for more mundane marketing vehicles like signs in stores, direct mail and even packaging that might hold consumers' attention for minutes or hours.

TD 

''The chart starts the conversation about what medium really matters for a specific brand,'' said Rob Price, one of Eleven's founders and a co-chairman and creative director, as he takes a visitor to the agency's offices here through a typical Eleven sales pitch that skips the usual reel of television advertisements to focus on products like corporate videos that the agency has made for Kodak and corporate reports it has done for Williams-Sonoma.

It is not as though Eleven disdains television advertisements -- far from it. Rather, Mr. Price says, the company has elbowed its way into a profitable niche of a crowded market by being what he calls ''media agnostic.'' That means keeping egos -- and the desire to win advertising prizes -- at bay and homing in on those promotional outlets that will get the most bang for the client's buck, he said. It is an unsentimental approach, and one the founders contend sets Eleven off from all those hot Web shops and other specialized agencies that sprang up in the 1990's and went bust in 2001.

''If we had made the choice to be only a Web shop, a small ad agency or an integrated marketing firm, we wouldn't be here today,'' said Michael Borosky, vice president and a creative director, who left an interactive agency in 1999 to help found Eleven. ''We couldn't afford to be pigeonholed.''

To create Eleven, Mr. Borosky, with Jordan Warren -- a colleague at the formerly highflying Web shop CKS (which became MarchFirst and later filed for bankruptcy) -- joined Mr. Price and Paul Curtin, two former design directors at Goodby, Berlin, Silverstein & Company, the advertising agency known for its ''Got Milk?'' campaign. They opened for business April 1, 1999, the height of the Internet boom.

The four wanted to build an agency that was not just concerned with creating Web sites, producing big television campaigns or going public on the stock market but with focusing on brand strategy. They took the name from the film ''This Is Spinal Tap'' and the character Nigel Tufnel's explanation for why his amplifiers go to 11 settings instead of the traditional 10, ''It's one louder.''

In its first year, the agency snagged big Internet accounts including Petstore.com, which would become part of Pets.com (now defunct), and PeoplePC, which was bought by Earthlink. Then the Internet bubble burst, and times became tough.

''We didn't pay our dues in the beginning,'' Mr. Borosky said. ''We joke about having to go through another start-up phase in 2001. That year really forced us back into reality.''

The reality was that too many agencies were vying for too few clients with the same old pitch of ''integrated marketing services,'' by which they generally meant making certain the brand looked the same across television, print and the Internet.

''Sure, that should happen, but that's homogenization, not integration,'' Mr. Warren, Eleven's president and chief executive, said. ''Integration should be where the media works together as part of the overall brand's strategy. The media should feed off each other.'' Mr. Curtin is co-chairman and a creative director.

The agnostic strategy, coupled with the creative strengths of all four founders, has brought Eleven some big clients in the last two years, including Microsoft and Kodak's digital camera business. It has also lured small clients, like the financial software company Hyperion, based in Sunnyvale, Calif., who do not want to spend huge sums on television advertising.

Mr. Warren estimates Eleven's revenues will rise to $6 million this year -- equivalent to $40 million in ''billings,'' the measure of performance favored by the industry -- from $4.9 million last year and $3.8 million in 2001.

Competitors say being small may make it easier for Eleven to practice its agnostic approach to media, but they agree that picking the best media for the client's brand -- not the one that will pay the most or stroke the biggest egos -- is really what agencies need to do. But it is not easy, especially for very large agencies with ingrained biases toward certain media. ''It's both a structural as well as philosophical change that has to happen,'' said Betsy Sperry, managing partner with Grey San Francisco, a unit of the Grey Global Group, which in the last year combined advertising, media buying, direct response and interactive into one profit-and-loss center to help it move to a more agnostic model. ''Old habits die hard, you have to make structural changes to break down those barriers,'' Ms. Sperry said.

Eleven is not afraid to try unusual methods to get its point across. To impress on Hyperion marketing executives the need to broaden their company's appeal beyond the technology-obsessed, it put the company's name on jargon-riddled advertisements it had lifted from trade magazines. The executives loved them -- at which point Eleven confessed to the subterfuge and said it was time for a change in an industry that was suffering huge losses as companies pulled back from technology spending.

Then it pitched its idea: spend big on a funny print campaign with a long text in The Wall Street Journal intended to reach chief executives and high-level technology executives who increasingly were making the purchasing decisions. One of the first advertisements would read, ''It is our considered opinion that software advertising ... is increasingly burdened with tired buzzwords and meaningless drivel.''

The suggested campaign and media purchase rang true with Hyperion's vice president for corporate marketing, Kathi Fox, who knew that chief executives and chief financial officers were making software decisions and that they disliked mumbo jumbo.

Attacking jargon also was at the heart of the strategy Eleven proposed for promoting Kodak's digital cameras. In 2000, most advertising for digital cameras was full of technical information and boring shots of the cameras. It played well with the first wave of enthusiasts, who ''love to dive into the technicalities,'' said Pierre Schaeffer, chief marketing officer for Kodak's digital-imaging division.

But the future lay in mainstream consumers, particularly women, who wanted ease of operation. Kodak had a product called EasyShare that fitted the bill, but it needed a marketing campaign to go with it. Eleven created a Web site, signs in stores, packaging and even stickers to go on the cameras that conveyed the point of simplicity by showing people using the camera. It also bucked convention by showing the camera from the back, not the front in all the marketing materials. ''The back of the camera is what people see when they take photos, it shows them the image they are capturing,'' Mr. Warren said.

The Eleven marketing campaign has been so successful -- Kodak credits Eleven's work with helping it become one of the top three digital camera brands with Sony and Olympus -- that its theme, simplicity, has been picked up by Kodak's primary agency, Ogilvy & Mather, for advertising. But Mr. Schaeffer of Kodak said Eleven's real success had been to refocus the brand, even if that made him uneasy at times. ''I remember it took a leap of faith for me to O.K. showing the back of the camera,'' he said. ''But now I realized that's where the journey begins for the consumer.''

Photo: The founders of the ad agency Eleven, Paul Curtin, left, and Rob Price, co-chairmen and creative directors; Michael Borosky, vice president and a creative director; and Jordan Warren, president and chief executive. (Photo by Peter DaSilva for The New York Times)

Chart: ''Surviving the Internet''

Eleven Inc. started as an ad agency catering mostly to Internet companies. But as many of those companies have failed, Eleven has adapted, finding new clients in more established industries.

April 1, 1999 -- Eleven opens with five clients, including Williams-Sonoma.

April 2, 1999 -- Lands new clients, including Petstore.com and Creditland.com.

Spring 2000 -- Loses Petstore.com and Creditland.com because of the Internet meltdown.

Spring 2001 -- Awarded a Kodak account.

Fall 2001 -- Hired by two former clients, Ameristar Casinos and Barclays Global Investors, as each company's principal agency.

Spring 2002 -- Awarded a Microsoft account.

Spring 2003 -- Awarded a Hyperion Solutions account.

HD Woven With a Woman's Touch And a Moroccan Sensibility
BY By ELAINE LOUIE
WC 146 words
PD 13 November 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 3
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

Sheva Fruitman, above, a photographer, went to Marrakesh two years ago and learned to weave airy textiles. She bought a loom, hired a weaver, Hammoud el-Foukahi, and began producing woven cotton panels suspended between sheer layers of polyester. The open weave of the panels, made in Marrakesh, makes them seem fragile, but she said they are surprisingly strong. Most Moroccan weavers are men, and she said that except for Mr. Foukahi, ''men don't want to work for a woman.'' So she trained two women to help. A panel 10 by 4 1/2 feet is $1,200 in cream, pale gray and pale blue. Shevafruitman@hotmail.com or (212) 925-5618. ELAINE LOUIE

A quarterly survey of corporate confidence showed that managers were more upbeat about the economy in the third quarter, a sign that next week's Bank of Japan Tankan survey will show major gains. Confidence rose fastest at large manufacturers. HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Investor Confidence Rises
BY By Petra Kappl (NYT)
WC 123 words
PD 10 December 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

Investor confidence in Germany rose to its highest in two and a half years last month, according to a survey. The index compiled by the ZEW Center for European Economic Research found that investor confidence rose to 73.4 percent in November from 67.2 percent in October. The institute said a Europewide index showed a similar increase. Economists said the optimism was based on faster economic growth in Germany, helped by the recovery in the United States. Another German research institute, DIW, raised its forecast for economic growth in the fourth quarter to 0.5 percent from 0.25 percent a day earlier. Petra Kappl (NYT)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTW
HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Business Confidence Rises
BY By Petra Kappl. (NYT)
WC 116 words
PD 19 December 2003
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2003 New York Times Company

LP 

An index of confidence in the German economy rose this month to its highest level in almost three years. The index, which is compiled by the Ifo Institute and measures the economic outlook among business executives, rose to 96.8 from 95.7 in November, its eighth consecutive monthly increase. The assessment of the current business climate rose only slightly, but the rating for future expectations was at a nine-year high, Ifo said. The president of the organization, Hans-Werner Sinn, said the report showed that ''the economic recovery has set in and is gaining strength.'' Petra Kappl. (NYT)

German business confidence climbed for the ninth consecutive month, according to a survey of 7,000 companies in western German, which was released by the Ifo Institute in Munich. SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD Index for February Shows Drop in Consumer Confidence
CR By Reuters
WC 292 words
PD 14 February 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 14
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

Consumer sentiment took an unexpectedly sharp fall in early February as Americans turned cautious over the economy, a survey showed yesterday.

The University of Michigan's preliminary reading of consumer sentiment tumbled to 93.1 this month from January's final reading of 103.8, which was its highest level in more than three years, according to market sources who saw the report.

TD 

That was way below economists' forecasts of 103.3 and confirmed market speculation that the figure would be sharply lower.

''It is kind of a stunner,'' said Chris Low, chief economist for FTN Financial in New York. ''A 10-point drop in the confidence index is significant and suggests there is a new pessimism among consumers that was not there a month ago. I suspect it has something to do with three surprisingly weak employment reports in a row.''

The current conditions index fell to 100.4 from 109.5 in January, while the expectations index dropped to 88.4 from a final reading of 100.1 last month.

Analysts say a wobbly jobs sector has dampened consumer confidence despite a strengthening economy.

Some, however, have cautioned against reading too much into the report, because it has no bearing on actual spending patterns of consumers. But the dip in consumer sentiment is troubling at this stage of the recovery, analysts agree. Consumer spending leads to business spending, which leads to more jobs.

The University of Michigan survey is based on telephone interviews with 500 households over the course of the month on personal finances and business and buying conditions.

The preliminary survey, released about midway through the month, is based on the first 250 interviews.

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD Job Worries Dim Outlook Of Consumers
CR By The Associated Press
WC 323 words
PD 25 February 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 5
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

Consumer confidence tumbled in February as persistent worries about jobs tarnished Americans' optimism, an industry group reported yesterday.

The Conference Board said its consumer confidence index dropped more than 9 points, to 87.3, after a rise in January to a revised reading of 96.4. That reading was the index's highest since mid-2002. Analysts expected a decline, but the index came in significantly below their consensus forecast of 92.3.

TD 

''Consumers began the year on a high note, but their optimism has quickly given way to caution,'' said Lynn Franco, director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center. ''Consumers remain disheartened with current economic conditions, and at the core of their disenchantment is the labor market.''

The confidence reading is watched closely because consumer spending accounts for two-thirds of the economy. The decline in confidence is a sign that consumers are disturbed by changes in the job market, which have quickly become a crucial issue in the presidential campaign, economists said.

''The labor market is actually improving, not very rapidly, but it's improving,'' said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at Wells Fargo & Company in Minneapolis. ''But the perception is otherwise.''

The decline in confidence reflects consumer uncertainty over both the current business climate and economic conditions in the next six months.

In February, an index measuring consumers' assessment of current conditions declined to 73.1, from 79.4 in January. Those describing the present climate as good declined to 19.3 percent, from 21.9 percent in January. Those who said conditions were bad increased to 25.1 percent, from 22.9 percent.

The sentiment on jobs was similarly depressed, with those saying positions are hard to get rising to 32.1 percent, from 31.6 percent, while those saying jobs are plentiful slipped to 11.8 percent, from 12.3 percent.

HD World Business Briefing Asia: Japan: Optimistic Economic Outlook
BY By Todd Zaun (NYT)
WC 132 words
PD 5 March 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

The Ministry of Finance said its survey of business sentiment showed that on balance big Japanese companies remained optimistic about the business environment for a second consecutive quarter after 10 straight periods of overall pessimism. Survey data also showed that business sentiment improved at small- and medium-size businesses in the first quarter, although those groups remained pessimistic about business conditions over all. The survey results suggest that Japan's strong economic performance for the fourth quarter of 2003 is continuing into 2004, economists said. A separate survey yesterday showed that Japanese companies increased capital investment by 5.1 percent in the fourth quarter of 2003, compared with the quarter a year earlier. Todd Zaun (NYT)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD Confidence Unexpectedly Shows a Rise
CR By BLOOMBERG NEWS
WC 461 words
PD 27 March 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 4
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

Consumer sentiment has unexpectedly risen this month, suggesting that tax refunds and an accelerating economy are giving Americans reasons for optimism even as job growth lags.

The University of Michigan's final index of consumer sentiment for March rose to 95.8, from 94.4 in February. Economists had expected a final reading of 93.7 in the gauge, according to the median of 52 economists in a Bloomberg News survey. The preliminary reading for March was 94.1.

TD 

Income tax rebates are up 5 percent on average from last year and forecasts call for the fastest pace of economic growth in two decades. That may be helping to buoy consumers until companies step up the pace of hiring. Declines in the stock market, a surge in gasoline prices and the March 11 terrorist attack in Madrid still weighed on attitudes.

''We suspect that soaring tax refunds are offsetting the Madrid effect, as well as the impact of higher gas prices and the dip in stocks,'' said Ian Shepherdson, chief United States economist at High Frequency Economics in Valhalla, N.Y. Rising sentiment ''will help perceptions of the strength of the recovery.''

A report from the Commerce Department yesterday said spending by consumers rose 0.2 percent in February, the fifth consecutive increase, after a 0.5 percent gain in January. Incomes rose 0.4 percent after a 0.3 percent rise in January.

The final Michigan index is based on a survey of about 500 households, double the sample size of the preliminary finding.

The survey's expectations index, which measures optimism about the next one to five months, rose to 88.8, from 88.5 a month earlier.

The current-conditions index, based on perceptions of consumers' financial situation and whether it is a good time to make major purchases, rose to 106.8, from 103.6 in February.

''Assuming that the nascent job market recovery builds some momentum, and that the equity market does not continue to head south, these indicators ought to remain on a generally improving path,'' said Joshua Shapiro, chief United States economist at MFR Inc. in New York.

The economy grew at an average annual pace of 6.1 percent in the final six months of 2003, and is forecast to expand 4.6 percent this year, the most since 1984, according to a Bloomberg News poll of economists.

HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Business Confidence Slips
BY By Petra Kappl (NYT)

German business confidence fell in March for a second consecutive month, raising doubts about the country's economic recovery and setting off calls for a reduction in interest rates. The Ifo Institute, which is based in Munich, said an index based on its survey of 7,000 businesses fell to a five-month low of 95.4, from 96.4 in February. Hans-Werner Sinn, president of the institute, said the decline showed that recovery in the German economy, which has been hurt by a high unemployment rate, was at risk. Petra Kappl (NYT)

HD Entrepreneurship Courses: It Pays to Pick the School
BY By JULIE FLAHERTY
WC 1267 words
PD 1 April 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 7
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

When Howard Anderson was a student at Harvard Business School in the late 1960's, it was a given that he and his classmates would graduate and take jobs as executives at Fortune 500 companies. His professors took a dim view of striking out on your own.

''They always treated entrepreneurs like there was something wrong with them,'' recalled Mr. Anderson, who, after a few ill-fated interviews with recruiters for corporations like the Ford Motor Company, started the Yankee Group, a high-technology market-research firm in Boston.

TD 

Today, Harvard flaunts its entrepreneurship program, as does the Sloan School of Management at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, where Mr. Anderson now teaches. Business schools in general have gone giddy with entrepreneurship. In 1970 there were 16 entrepreneurship programs nationwide; as of 2000, about 1,500 universities and colleges offered such courses. .

By the numbers, this should be the best time in history to learn about starting a small business. But what an entrepreneurial education means depends a lot on the school.

Top-tier business schools like Harvard and M.I.T. emphasize the entrepreneurial mind-set rather than the details of being a small-business owner. The nuts and bolts of keeping a small venture going are left to the community colleges and Small Business Administration programs. And somewhere in between are schools that instill their entrepreneurship programs with a healthy dose of small-business basics.

Students who come to the Sloan School of Management are not looking to be ''lifestyle entrepreneurs,'' who are small-business owners content to keep their businesses healthy but not necessarily wealthy. ''We want them to aim higher,'' Mr. Anderson said. About 15 percent of his students have patents in their own names, and while their ventures may start out small, few intend to stay that way.

One reason to separate entrepreneurship from small-business management, Mr. Anderson said, is that founders and managers are often different species. Although he himself founded and ran the Yankee Group for 30 years, he does not see himself as the norm.

''Once a company is up and running it takes a different set of expertise,'' he said. ''Very few people are both the entrepreneur and the guy to run it for the next 40 years.''

Mr. Anderson does teach practicalities, of course. M.I.T.'s entrepreneurship classes focus on all the crucial steps of a venture, like putting out the fires that start-ups face. For example, what happens if your product does not work, or what happens if your partners quit on you?

At the George Washington University's School of Business and Public Management, some students intend to be the next Bill Gates. Others are simply preparing to take over the family business.

''We try to imbue in our students entrepreneurial spirit and then couple that with good small-business management,'' said Charles N. Toftoy, director of the school's entrepreneurship and small-business program.

Dr. Toftoy teams his undergraduate and graduate students with small to medium-size businesses in the Washington area, from hair salons to software companies, where they help the owners solve their biggest problems. They have assisted more than 2,100 companies with issues like competitor analysis, cash-flow management and inventory control.

''We've helped businesses arrange their parking spaces,'' Dr. Toftoy said.

As business majors at George Washington, Michael Goldman and four other students were assigned to help a local Salvadoran restaurant. The owner's first request was that the students translate the menu into English. ''Then she wanted us to figure out a way to make more money,'' Mr. Goldman said. The team helped her place advertisements in the local Spanish-language newspaper and recruit people to hand out coupons.

Although Mr. Goldman, now 23 and a real estate broker at the Ezra Company in Bethesda, Md., has no interest in starting his own restaurant, the experience gave him a full notebook to consult should he some day start his own company.

One reason schools may overlook the small-business side of entrepreneurship is that few small-business owners go back to school for a business degree. Most simply do not have the time. In 1997, Charles Kendall signed up as a full-time M.B.A. student at George Washington, but as the owner of a small video-production business, the founder of a fledgling video-equipment company and a father expecting his second child, he had one too many sleepless nights to continue past the first year.

Mr. Kendall, 35, who is now a father of three and owner of Moon Bounce Media in Herndon, Va., and Spider Support Systems in Reston, Va., returned to the university in 2000, this time ''going real slow'' with one or two courses each semester. As he expands his businesses, he appreciates both sides of the entrepreneurship and small-business program.

Mr. Kendall said his father, who turned his own small business into a company with 4,000 employees, inspired him. ''He prodded me to get the M.B.A.,'' he said. ''It has opened my eyes to the opportunities.''

Even without enrolling at a business school, small-business owners have traditionally had plenty of informal ways to get an education. Colleges of all sizes offer certificate programs and continuing education classes geared toward small-business owners. In addition, the Small Business Administration sponsors Small Business Development Centers at 1,100 locations, many of them at colleges and universities.

Smaller educational institutions are building on those relationships. Millikin University in Decatur, Ill., has worked for years with local small businesses through the on-campus chapter of the Service Corps of Retired Executives, a partner of the Small Business Administration. In the 1990's the university's Tabor School of Business began to translate that experience into its curriculum by offering classes like small-business consulting and entrepreneurial management strategy, and in 1998 a Center for Entrepreneurship was founded.

Millikin is developing an entrepreneurship and art program, where art, music, theater and dance students learn about turning their talents into personal businesses. A cornerstone of the program is the Blue Connection, a commercial art gallery that sells works created by Millikin students, faculty and alumni.

''We don't have an engineering department, but we do have a wonderful school of fine arts,'' said Sharon T. Alpi, an assistant professor of business administration and the director of the entrepreneurship center. ''It made sense to us to look at how we can integrate entrepreneurship not only in the business school, but how can we make it part of Millikin as a whole.''

The Ewing Marion Kauffman Foundation, an organization in Kansas City, Mo., that encourages entrepreneurship, is also pushing to make entrepreneurship classes more accessible.

A majority of American entrepreneurs do not come out of business schools, said Carl J. Schramm, the foundation's president and chief executive, and, yet, unless you are a business major, ''The hardest place to take a course is often in the business school.''

With that in mind, the foundation recently awarded $25 million in grants to eight universities that pledged to make entrepreneurial education available across their campus, not just to business majors. Plans range from making an entrepreneurship minor available to all undergraduate students at Howard University in Washington to creating an Entrepreneurial Academy at Florida International University in Miami.

Photo: George Washington University students discuss what they can do as consultants to help a company. (Photo by Marty Katz for The New York Times)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTW
HD In Japan, Confidence In a Recovery Is Spreading
BY By TODD ZAUN
WC 620 words
PD 2 April 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

TOKYO, April 1 -- Executives at Japan's biggest manufacturers are more optimistic about business than at any time in nearly seven years, and the mood is spreading among smaller companies, a survey by the central bank showed on Thursday.

The results of the Bank of Japan's closely watched Tankan survey of 10,562 companies showed that the two-year-old recovery, so far driven by strong overseas demand for Japanese cars, electronics and machinery, is seeping into other sectors.

TD 

The survey showed improved confidence among service providers and smaller companies that rely on domestic consumer demand, which has shown signs of resurgence.

The survey was seen as further evidence that this recovery has more staying power than two short-lived upturns in the last decade that were driven initially by exports but dried up when consumers at home failed to spend.

''The survey shows the Japanese economy is expanding broadly,'' said Mamoru Yamazaki, chief economist at Barclays Capital (Japan) Ltd. ''I believe the economy will accelerate, particularly in the second half.''

The quarterly survey's benchmark subindex of large manufacturers, which includes about 1,200 companies, rose to plus 12 in March, from plus 7 in December. The latest figure was the highest since June 1997, when the index was plus 13, and was the fourth consecutive increase.

The index for large nonmanufacturers rose to plus 5 in March, from zero in December as retailers, real estate companies and other service providers grew more optimistic.

The central bank's indexes measure business sentiment by subtracting the percentage of companies that say business conditions are unfavorable from those that say they are favorable. Economists say growing optimism in the service sector reflects expectations for more consumer spending, which accounts for about 60 percent of Japan's gross domestic product. Reports of recent pickups in household spending and retail sales are reinforcing that view.

The growing confidence in Japan's recovery has helped lift the benchmark Nikkei 225 stock average 9.4 percent so far this year. The Nikkei closed at 11,683.42 on Thursday, down 0.27 percent.

One caveat economists noted is that the survey was completed before the yen surged against the dollar this week. A rising yen hurts Japanese exporters, like Toyota and Sony, by reducing the value of their dollar-denominated profits when changed into yen.

Big companies in the survey forecast an average exchange rate of 108.43 yen per dollar for the fiscal year ending March 2005, but the yen has already strengthened beyond that, trading at 103.73 to the dollar on Thursday from as weak as 112 last month.

Still, despite the improved sentiment at smaller manufacturers and service providers, companies in those categories remained overwhelmingly pessimistic. The survey's index for small companies improved to minus 3 in March from minus 10 in December.

Many economists say Japanese companies still face challenges, especially smaller companies that depend on demand at home. While the country's long-deteriorating job market seems to be stabilizing, there are few signs that wages are rising, a necessity for a healthy recovery in consumer demand.

''The sustainability of the current recovery is still uncertain,'' said Shuji Shirota, an economist at Dresdner Kleinwort Wasserstein in Tokyo.

Perhaps reflecting the uncertainty about exchange rates and the strength of consumer demand, large companies surveyed did not see conditions continuing to improve. Large manufacturers forecast their index would hold steady at plus 12 for June, the same level as in March.

Photo: A pickup in consumer demand in Japan has helped to improve the confidence among smaller companies. (Photo by Associated Press)(pg. W7)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD Service Industries Index Climbs to a Record
CR By Bloomberg News
WC 461 words
PD 6 April 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 4
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

A gauge of service industries rose unexpectedly sharply to a record in March as orders and employment climbed, and a quarterly survey of chief executives showed that their confidence in the economy is the highest in more than 20 years, reports released yesterday showed.

The Institute for Supply Management's index of financial services, construction, retail and other nonmanufacturing enterprises rose to 65.8 in March from 60.8 the month before, exceeding the previous record of 65.7 in January.

TD 

Economists had expected the index to rise to 61.5. Readings higher than 50 indicate expansion in the report, which started in 1997.

The measure of services industries, which was higher than all 54 forecasts in a Bloomberg News survey of economists, builds on the government report Friday that the economy added 308,000 jobs last month, all of them in nonmanufacturing businesses.

The reports have some investors contemplating whether the Federal Reserve will increase interest rates and some economists considering whether to raise their projections of growth for the second quarter.

''This is a very strong report -- it's confirmation of the big rise in payrolls we saw on Friday, and it's a sign that consumers are spending,'' said Chris Low, chief economist at FTN Financial in New York.

In a separate report, the Conference Board's index of business confidence rose to 73 in the first quarter, from 66 in last year's final three months.

The reading was the highest since 74 in the last quarter of 1983. Half of the more than 100 chief executives surveyed said they expected to hire more workers this year.

The purchasing managers' report showed that its index of prices paid, a measure of costs for purchased materials and services, surged to 65.7 from 57.3 in February. The increase was the biggest since the index began in August 1997. Sixteen out of 17 industry groups reported increased costs.

''The prices-paid component may start to ring some inflation alarm bells, which is a theme that's going to gather momentum as we go along this year,'' Mr. Low said.

Stephen Stanley, chief economist at RBS Greenwich Capital in Greenwich, Conn., said, ''The vigor of the economic recovery extends beyond the manufacturing sector.''

''The stage has been set for a re-acceleration'' in the second quarter, he said, to an annual growth rate of about 5.5 percent from about 4 percent at an annual pace from January through March.

The survey's employment index rose to 53.9 from 52.7, the sixth consecutive month of growth. The group's March manufacturing survey, released on Thursday, showed the highest employment index since December 1987.

HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Investor Confidence Slips
BY By Petra Kappl (NYT)

SN The New York Times
SC NYTF

LP 

German investor confidence fell for a fourth month in April, reflecting concern over the sluggish economic recovery. The ZEW Center for European Economic Research reported the decline in its index measuring the sentiment of institutional investors and analysts. The index has fallen every month since hitting a three-and-a-half year high in December. According to Germany's central bank, the economy grew 0.25 percent in the first quarter, at about the same level as in the two previous quarters. In reaction to the ZEW report, the euro fell nearly 1 percent against the dollar. Petra Kappl (NYT)

LP 

German business confidence rose for the first time in three months in April, as the recent weakness of the euro against the dollar and the growing global economy brightened the outlook for exporters. The Ifo Institute of Munich said its confidence index, based on a survey of 7,000 executives, climbed to 96.3, from 95.4 in March. Economists had forecast a slight decline. The euro has declined about 8 percent since reaching a record $1.293 on Feb. 18, reviving sentiment among exporters. Germany has relied on sales abroad as rising unemployment has deterred consumer spending in Europe.

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTW
HD Japan Survey Puts Optimism At the Highest In 12 Years
BY By TODD ZAUN
WC 656 words
PD 2 July 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

TOKYO, July 1 -- Business is better than at any time since the collapse of the bubble economy a dozen years ago, according to a survey of Japanese companies released Thursday by the central bank.

The surprisingly strong results of the Bank of Japan's closely watched Tankan survey of some 10,000 companies showed that the two-year economic recovery remains on track. A wide range of businesses -- from conglomerates to restaurants -- said they were optimistic about their prospects, which reinforces the view that recovery has spread beyond exports, the main driver of growth so far.

TD 

''The Tankan suggests that this indeed is the strongest recovery in the post-bubble era,'' said Ryo Hino, an economist at J.P. Morgan Securities Asia. ''It confirms that business conditions continue to improve and that the improvement is widespread.''

The quarterly survey's benchmark index of large manufacturers, which includes about 1,200 companies, rose to plus 22 in June, from plus 12 in March. The index measures sentiment by subtracting the percentage of companies that say conditions are unfavorable from those that say they are favorable. A positive score means optimists outnumber pessimists. The latest figure was the highest since August 1991, and was the fifth consecutive increase.

Among the most optimistic companies were steel makers, which have experienced growth thanks to strong demand from Chinese factories.

The index for large nonmanufacturers rose to plus 9 in June, from plus 5 in March, and was the best score since February 1992, as restaurants and transportation companies grew more optimistic.

Growing confidence among smaller companies and service providers, which depend closely on domestic demand, was seen as especially encouraging because it suggests a recovery in consumption in Japan. That element was missing from two earlier recoveries that fizzled out in the last decade as initial bursts of export growth faded.

The survey also showed that companies planned to invest more. Big businesses said that they planned to increase investment an average of 5.7 percent in the fiscal year ending in March 2005, compared with an average 0.6 percent cut in investment that they planned just three months ago.

The rosy survey results follow a period of robust expansion for Japan. The economy grew at an annual 6.1 percent in the first quarter after expanding 7.3 percent in the last quarter of 2003.

Despite the solid numbers, some economists still harbor doubts about how much longer Japan's growth spurt will last. Growth for the economy as a whole still depends to a great extent on exports to China and the United States, leaving Japan vulnerable to a slowdown in the United States and to attempts by Chinese central bankers to slow that country's expansion.

A rise in the yen against the dollar could also take some of the steam out of the recovery as a stronger Japanese currency would make exports less profitable.

Also, there are few signs that ordinary Japanese consumers share the optimism of the country's businesses. The job market has been slowly improving but unemployment among the young remains high. And though Japanese corporations have become much more profitable in recent years, many of the gains have come at the expense of ordinary workers who have seen their wages fall.

And Thursday's survey presented a mixed outlook for the months ahead. Large manufacturers expect business conditions to deteriorate slightly in the next three months.

''Firms are expecting a higher yen, which is one of the risk factors for the economy,'' said Shuji Shirota, an economist at Dresdner Kleinwort Wasserstein in Tokyo. ''We expect some adjustment phase for the Japanese economic recovery early next year.''

Photo: A survey shows that exports driven by a demand for electronics and more spending by Japanese are strengthening Japan's economy. (Photo by Associated Press)(pg. W7)

CLM SMALL BUSINESS
SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
HD A Chance to Sell Among the Giants
BY By ELIZABETH OLSON
WC 1453 words
PD 8 July 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 2
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

George Killingsworth thought he had discovered a promising product to supplement the earnings from his struggling sign-painting business -- award plaques made from sandblasted alder wood.

Mr. Killingsworth created the plaques in his sign shop near Pearland, Tex. People who saw them raved about the sand carving and laser work that included designs like the silhouette of a state or a golfer, and he started selling them to nearby trophy shops.

TD 

But like a lot of entrepreneurs, he lacked the resources and expertise to market to a wider audience. Then, in short order, he received help from an initiative started last year by the Small Business Administration to give entrepreneurs access to contacts that would normally be out of reach. Called Business Matchmaking, the program invites small-business owners to meet with representatives of big corporations and government agencies at events the S.B.A. organizes around the country.

In December, a friend mentioned to Mr. Killingsworth that one such event was taking place in Houston. He had never heard of the program, but he sensed opportunity.

After scrambling to line up four appointments with prospective clients who would be attending, he tossed several plaques into his Chevy pickup and drove the 20 minutes to the George R. Brown Convention Center in Houston, the site of the event. He had heard that he should dress up, so he bought a new pair of shoes and a sports coat to wear over his golf shirt, ''so I looked like a used car salesman,'' he recalled.

Whatever impression his attire made, he received instant business gratification. The S.B.A. cautions participants not to expect a signed contract immediately, telling them that the interviews are mostly get-acquainted sessions. But unlike service companies, his REK Products Inc. had a product to show, and he was able to line up a $5,000 contract the first day. No sooner had he signed it, he said, than he bumped into Hector V. Barreto Jr., the S.B.A. director, who was making the rounds.

''He cautioned me not to expect a contract immediately,'' Mr. Killingsworth said with a chuckle. ''Then I told him that I just wrote up an order.''

That was fine with the S.B.A. chief, who has high hopes for the program. ''We're taking procurement out of the Beltway and bringing it to Main Street,'' Mr. Barreto said in an interview.

The Hewlett-Packard Company helps pay for the events, which have been held in 10 cities so far, including Anaheim, Calif.; Birmingham, Ala.; Chicago; Houston and Orlando, Fla., with another scheduled next month in Philadelphia.

In Orlando, 500 small-business owners made pitches to 130 buyers. Over all, nearly 5,000 small businesses have attended the conferences and have held close to 19,000 meetings with prospective buyers. S.B.A. officials say they can track only contracts that are awarded on the spot, which have totaled about $17 million, but they think the value of all deals is far higher because multiple meetings are usually required before contracts are signed.

Mr. Barreto wants to make the matchmaking program permanent, and the agency is looking for ways to finance it when it expires at the end of the year. ''We love this program,'' an S.B.A. spokesman, Seth Becker, said. ''We are going to do whatever we can to keep it going.''

One reason is that it gives entrepreneurs a shot at business that they otherwise might not even know about. The Small Business Act requires that about 23 percent of government contracts for goods and services go to small businesses. In June, the S.B.A. reported that a record $65.5 billion, or 23.6 percent, went to small ventures in fiscal 2003, up from $53 billion in 2002.

Even so, many small businesses complain of the difficulties of landing deals with federal agencies. Part of the problem is that many contracts bundle a broad range of goods and services that only big suppliers can provide. Another, said Mr. Barreto, who was an entrepreneur in California before assuming the agency's leadership, is the difficulty most small companies have in getting the ear of Washington bureaucrats. It is that gap that he is trying to address with the matchmaking project.

''Our job is to empower 25 million businesses across the country,'' Mr. Barreto said.

A worthy goal, but opinions on the agency's efforts still vary widely. ''They appear to be opening doors for small contractors, and I want to help the agency build on this progress,'' said Senator Olympia J. Snowe, the Maine Republican who is chairwoman of the Senate Committee on Small Business and Entrepreneurs.

But critics, who pounced on the S.B.A. earlier this year for the temporary shutdown of a popular loan program, and who more generally complain about what they see as its excessive bureaucracy, are not so sure.

Representative Nydia M. Velazquez, the New York Democrat on the House Small Business Committee, for example, wonders if the program is nothing more than a glorified trade show.

''Some $1.7 million in funding has been diverted from S.B.A. programs that are specifically aimed at helping low-income and minority businesses to fund the initiative,'' she said. The S.B.A. claims the true figure is $1 million.

Ms. Velazquez said the S.B.A. had not provided hard data on whether the initiative was actually working, and whether this money was increasing contract opportunities for small businesses.

It works for some. Marcus Mosley made a spur-of-the-moment 20-minute drive from his family's business in Gary, Ind. -- Champs Doors and Windows -- to a matchmaking session in Chicago last June and won a $200,000 deal to replace doors at local Navy installations. And Donnie McDaniel, head of Best Products in Arlington, Tex., signed a contract at the Houston session with a Halliburton division for $90,000 in construction items for Iraq.

Without the program, Mr. McDaniel said, he would never have gotten a foot in the door. ''To do business with a Lockheed Martin or a Raytheon or any large government prime, it almost takes an act of Congress,'' he said. The chance for one-on-one meetings with vendors for big corporations and government agencies, albeit usually for just the 15 minutes that are generally allotted, he said, ''is an opportunity we won't pass up.''

Dan Sturdivant, who gives out information about contracts for the Department of Homeland Security, said the matchmaking sessions ''help contractors learn their homework.''

''We tell them what contracts are coming up,'' Mr. Sturdivant said, ''what the process is, and help facilitate the relationship -- it works for everybody.''

Mr. Barreto imported the matchmaking idea from Los Angeles, where he led the Latin Business Association, California's largest Hispanic business organization. Using the same model of bringing together corporations and government agencies with small businesses, association members landed about $15 million in contracts in 1999. Last year, that number grew to $100 million, all in the Los Angeles area.

Determined to use that experience to take the mystery out of the procurement process, Mr. Barreto decided to train and counsel small companies so they were able to present their products and services so the big agencies and companies had a thorough understanding of what they had to offer.

Much of that work is done online, at www.businessmatchmaking.com[http://www.businessmatchmaking.com], where companies can register their information. Aided by a group of retired executives, the S.B.A. estimates that it trained and counseled about 2.1 million businesses last year, and about 40 percent of them were handled online.

During business-matchmaking events, Hewlett-Packard sponsors a cybercafe where entrepreneurs can stop between appointments for information and advice from lending institutions.

Mr. Killingsworth, who signed two contracts at the Houston event, declined to give details on how his award-plaque business was doing, except to say that ''we are running ahead of last year because we are doing more retail, which means we are selling directly and getting a better price.''

The best thing about the experience, he says, is not the money; it is the self-confidence he has gained.

''I had a lot of people looking at the product,'' he said, ''and saying that it looked great, so I learned a lot about selling it.''

Photo: George Killingsworth of Pearland, Tex., tossed several wooden plaques into his Chevy pickup, drove to Houston and lined up a $5,000 contract at a Small Business Administration matchmaking event. (Photo by Michael Stravato for The New York Times)

HD World Business Briefing Europe: Germany: Business Confidence Rises
BY By Victor Homola (NYT)
WC 109 words

SN The New York Times
SC NYTF

PG 1
LA English

German business confidence rose in July, reversing two months of declines, according to a survey of 7,000 companies in western Germany. The index, released by the Ifo Institute in Munich, rose to 95.6, from 94.6 in June, indicating that the economy is continuing to gain momentum. Economists had forecast a rise to 95.2. Views of the current situation and expectations for the coming six months both rose; optimism was particularly striking in wholesaling and retailing. But the climate in the construction sector continued a downward trend. Victor Homola (NYT)

HD World Business Briefing Asia: Japan: Consumer Confidence Rises
BY By Todd Zaun (NYT)
WC 129 words
PD 11 August 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

A gauge of Japanese consumer sentiment rose to its highest level in 13 years in July as the outlook for jobs and for income growth improved, the government said. The nationwide consumer confidence index rose to 48.7 in July, from 44.9 in June. The July score was the highest since the index hit 49.4 in June of 1991. The improvement in confidence bodes well for Japan's two-year economic recovery. While the rebound has been driven mostly by rising exports so far, signs of growing consumption, which accounts for more than half of the economy, are providing hope the recovery will continue into a third year.

Todd Zaun (NYT)

SE Business/Financial Desk; SECTW
HD Amid Turmoil, an Economic Pickup
BY By JUAN FORERO
WC 1486 words
PD 12 August 2004
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 1
LA English
CY (c) 2004 New York Times Company

LP 

CARACAS, Venezuela, Aug. 11 -- With its protests and political tumult of the last few years, Venezuela has been anything but hospitable to business.

But at sprawling car plants in the city of Valencia, southwest of Caracas, production this year is up sharply. Eli Lilly & Company, maker of impotence drugs, Prozac and insulin, is expecting sales to shoot up. The DirectTV Group says the number of subscribers should increase 18 percent this year, to 320,000.

TD 

And Juan Vicente Alvarez, whose family has had a construction company for 34 years, is planning a string of apartment complexes that are its largest projects since 1999.

It is all part of an economic rebound that, economists say, could lead to growth rates of 10 to 12 percent this year, nearly double the government's initial forecasts. The rise in Venezuela's economy, Latin America's fourth largest, is helping to drive what the United Nations says will be a 4 percent expansion in Latin America this year, the biggest since 1997.

For Venezuela's left-leaning government, which has been fending off threats to remain in power, the economy's rebound could not have come a moment too soon.

Facing a recall referendum on Sunday that could end President Hugo Chavez's turbulent five years in office, the economic boom -- driven by soaring oil prices that have left Venezuela awash in petro-dollars -- is spurring heavy government spending on social programs that are having the desired effect: solidifying support when the president needs it most.

But while the government celebrates, Venezuela's economy is anything but healthy, after a two-year contraction that some economists call one of the biggest free falls in Latin America in years. In 2002, the economy fell 8.9 percent, and crumpled again in 2003, by 9.4 percent.

Crippled by antigovernment strikes in 2002 that went into 2003, production at Venezuela's large government-owned oil company at one point slowed to a trickle. Companies went out of business, and many people lost their jobs, floating into the country's mushrooming informal economy.

With only one way to go -- up -- the economy has registered startling growth this year.

''It's a statistical bounce since it was going to come back,'' explained Rafael de la Fuente, chief Latin economist for BNP Paribas in New York. ''Still, you're not back at the level you were two years ago. All told, this is still an economy that hasn't recovered.''

Driven by world oil prices that are approaching $45 a barrel, oil revenues have risen 120 percent in last 12 months, and nonoil revenue has nearly doubled, LatinSource, a network of economists based in New York, said in a recent report.

The economic indicators, though, gloss over an economy unable to attract much foreign investment or create jobs for the 50 percent of Venezuelan laborers who toil in the informal sector, selling on the street or running unregulated businesses. Hamstrung by stringent exchange controls and an unpredictable political situation, many local businesses have been unable to grow.

''Clearly what you're seeing is a normalization of activity at quite a low level,'' said Michael Gavin, chief economist for UBS in Stamford, Conn. ''They recovered from the heart attack, but the cancer is there.''

Indeed, economic indicators show that Venezuela attracted only $1.4 billion in foreign direct investment in 2003, down from $4.3 billion in 1998, according to Mr. Gavin's calculations.

The central government is also running deficits as spending rises to $26.9 billion this year from $24.3 billion last year, according to estimates by Credit Suisse First Boston. The budget deficit is expected to be about 5.9 percent of gross domestic product this year, First Boston said, and rise to 6.7 percent next year. The gap was less than 2 percent of gross domestic product in 1999.

''The current fiscal stance is unsustainable,'' said the bank in a report on Aug. 6, explaining that although high oil prices have permitted more spending, the government will be hit hard when prices drop. The report, written after a visit to Venezuela, said that First Boston researchers ''saw nothing during our visit to change our broadly negative long-term view of Venezuela.''

The Chavez government dismisses such assessments, saying that its social spending generates jobs and that the country's fiscal condition is healthy. Venezuela is, indeed, well regarded for paying its debts on time, and its international reserves have shot up to nearly $24 billion, from $15 billion in 1998.

''This is a government that shows it can come back,'' said Ricardo Dorado, vice minister of labor, explaining that much of the country's free fall was brought about by opposition groups. ''There is now a recovery.''

Signs of a comeback can be found in sectors like auto production, retail sales and, little by little, construction, which is taking its first baby steps after lean years when building practically reached a standstill.

The Ford Motor Company in Valencia said that about 140 cars and trucks were being built each day -- about 50 percent more than last year -- by 1,600 workers, 450 more than last year.

In all, the automobile industry in this land of big cars and cheap gas may build 120,000 cars and trucks this year, twice the number of 2003. It falls far short of the 217,000 vehicles made in 2001, but executives say they are pleased nevertheless.

''For Ford, this has been an excellent year,'' said Enmanuel Cassingena, president of Ford Andina, which also oversees production in Colombia and Ecuador. With its new compact cars, Ford has a 21.7 percent market share in Venezuela, 5.6 percent more than at this point last year, Mr. Cassingena said.

''The sector has rebounded faster than we had thought,'' he said.

In Caracas, other sectors that have registered upticks include construction, up 19.5 percent in the first quarter; retailing, up 27.9 percent, and transport and storage, which grew 25.3 percent. Manufacturing over all, including car production, increased 50 percent in the first three months.

Jorge Botti, who represents retailers and runs several businesses, said his 47-year-old family-run company, a hardware wholesaler called Padlock, has expanded into several business lines. He provides high-technology hardware to the telecommunications industry and is opening a big food wholesaler.

''It has flourished,'' Mr. Botti said of his business. ''We are being reborn.''

But under the rosy surface, he explained, is an ailing economy. Some big competitors have gone out of business, a trend seen across the country and leading to a concentration of companies. And he notes that the encouraging economic data this year hide the fact that the growth rates are being compared with the period last year, when the economy was paralyzed and near collapse.

''What we have is a debacle -- we have more people and a smaller economy,'' he said. ''The government holds it all up as a success, but what you see is lost business and a lack of competitors.''

Construction, considered a barometer of the economy, is also in a delicate position. Companies are building schools in the government's drive to bolster education. A metro system, is being built in Los Teques, outside Caracas, and in the capital the subway is being expanded. Some big projects, like a bridge over the Orinoco River and a major highway, are also moving forward.

Mr. Alvarez, the builder whose company was started in 1970, is embarking on an upscale complex of 300 apartments, called Sun Views, in Caracas and a project of 700 more units in Valencia. But Mr. Alvarez, who also represents realtors, says that is a tepid start.

In 2003, only 9,000 apartment units and houses were built, down from 92,000 in 1997, according to the leading builders' association in Caracas. In all, construction in 2003 accounted for a third of the economic activity it generated in 2001. The industry has 550,000 workers, down from 1.1 million during the 1970's construction boom.

Now, with the economy reviving, Mr. Alvarez is somewhat optimistic that his and other companies will soon see better days. But he says there are hurdles of all kinds, from more cautious buyers to a lack of credit to political uncertainty.

''There are projects that are ready to get off the ground,'' he said. ''But, of course, the purchasing power of people is not where it should be.''

W1); Higher oil prices have helped lift Venezuela, but the economy has had problems attracting foreign investors and creating jobs for laborers. (Photo by David Rochkind/Polaris, for The New York Times)(pg. W7)

German business confidence slipped slightly in August, a monthly survey by the Munich-based Ifo Institute said. The overall index, based on a survey of 7,000 companies which were asked about the current business climate, was 95.3 in August compared with 95.6 in July, the third decline in four months. Nevertheless, the decline was not as sharp as feared. In the manufacturing, wholesale and retail sectors, sentiment worsened slightly, while in the construction sector the climate improved a bit. Victor Homola (NYT)
